Therefore, in the general case s ( w ) take the form given in Eq. ( 4 . 10 ) but can be simplified if interactions between the mobile particles are neglected. Then, the velocity cross-correlation term GLYPH&lt;229&gt; N i = j 〈 vi ( 0 ) · vj ( t ) 〉 = 0 and s ( w ) is simply related to D ( w ) via the Nernst-Einstein equation :

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

For interacting particles however, the cross-correlation does not vanish and Eq. ( 4 . 12 ) had to be modified. It is usually rewritten as :

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where H ( w ) is the frequency dependent Haven ratio measuring the strength of the cross correlation at different frequencies.

Finally, in the specific case where particles move via uncorrelated random jumps, that is if they have no memory, the velocity selfcorrelation term is a proportional to a delta function at time t = 0. The Fourier transform of a delta function in the time domain yields a constant function in the frequency domain. Therefore, uncorrelated random particle hopping is a special case where conductivity and tracer diffusion coefficient are frequency independent. This behavior is usually not observed except at low frequencies where the time scale is long enough for memory effects ( i.e. correlations) to vanish. Again the main challenge to fit experimental s ( w ) is to find a model describing the ionic motion in order to find an appropriate description for 〈 GLYPH&lt;229&gt; i , j vi ( 0 ) · vj ( t ) 〉 .

## 4 . 2 models for ionic conduction

## 4 . 2 . 1 Debye relaxation

Debye relaxation was first derived to describe the relaxation of polarization in a dielectric after an electric field is applied.[ 22 ] While an ionic conductor does not contain permanent dipoles, the jump of a charge carrier is analogous to the rotation of a dipole, as shown in Fig. 4 . 1 .

Figure 4 . 1 : Comparison between the rotation of a dipole and the jump of a cation.

The Debye relaxation model can thus also serve as a basis to describe relaxation in ionic conductors. In that model, it is assumed that there is no memory of previous excitations, that is the variation of polarization is simply proportional to the polarization and the proportionality factor is a constant relaxation rate:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Therefore, the variation of the polarization as a function of time is described by the simple expression:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

F ( t ) is the relaxation function already introduced in Eq. ( 4 . 1 ) and often written as exp -( t / t ) where t = 1/ W 0 is the characteristic relaxation time. It also carries the temperature dependence of the relaxation:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

It was shown in Eq. ( 4 . 5 ) that M ( w ) = 1 # ¥ i w F ( w ) . For the Debye relaxation we thus get:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

From Table 3 . 1 we also have, s ( w ) = i w# 0/ M ( w ) = # 0 # ¥ / ( F ( w )) -1 which gives:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Therefore, the Debye model results in a constant, frequency independent real conductivity while the imaginary part of the electric modulus is a Lorentzian with asymptotic w and w -1 dependence. These features are identical to the ones derived from an ideal RC circuit model and shown in Fig. 3 . 2 a and we get the relations:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

which relate the resistance and capacitance of the circuit to materials properties.

## 4 . 2 . 2 Non-Debye relaxation

As mentioned in Section 3 . 2 . 4 , experimental conductivity often does not follow the ideal behavior described by a RC circuit or by the Debye model. While these predict a frequency independent real conductivity, a frequency dependence is almost always observed in fast ion conductors. In 1977 , Jonscher formulated this universal dielectric response with a power law dependence of the real conductivity as [ 20 ]:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

In terms of an equivalent circuit, this behavior is also often modeled by replacing the capacitor by an imperfect capacitor, the so called constant phase element (CPE) with ZCPE = ( i w C ) 1 -n , 0 ≤ n &lt; 1. The CPE is usually used to fit a depressed semicircle in a Nyquist plot, which is related to the frequency dependent conductivity. In the case where n = 0 the case of an ideal RC circuit is recovered. However, both the Jonscher expression or the CPE leads to an nonphysical, diverging M ′′ ( w ) at high frequencies for n &gt; 0.[ 23 ]

An alternative description to the deviation from the Debye model considers that the electric field decay is better described by the so called Kohlrausch-Williams-Watts (KWW) function[ 24 , 25 ]:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where t ∗ is the KWW relaxation time. Physically, this equation can be understood by considering that the moving ions in the lattice experience a static or dynamic change in the local environment due to the presence of nonequivalent lattice sites, and/or ion-ion interactions. Mathematically, Eq. ( 4 . 22 ) is equivalent to a superposition of simple exponential, i. e. a superposition of Debye processes with different relaxation times:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where g ( t ) represents a distribution of relaxation times. The problem now lies in finding an appropriate explanation for the distribution of relaxation times.

Several models have been derived to explain such non-Debye relaxation.[ 26 ] Some are modification of the random walk model and consider the jump of single particles as in the conductivity formalism. These models include for example the Continuous Time Random Walk model that adds a waiting time distribution between jumps of particles,[ 27 ] or the Random Barrier model where the hopping charge

carriers experience randomly varying energy barriers.[ 28 ] While these models do not explicitly considers particle interactions, the Jump Relaxation Model includes the effect of a jumping ion on its surrounding. It includes Coulomb interactions in an effective potential experienced by the moving ion. The result is a non-zero probability for a correlated backward jump due to the Coulomb repulsion, which adds a contribution to the velocity autocorrelation function and thus can explain a frequency dependent conductivity.[ 29 ]

Other models follow an approach related to the electric modulus formalism and try to explain the deviation of the electric relaxation from a single exponential. Similarly to the Jump Relaxation Model, The Coupling Model (CM) considers a slowing down of the relaxation time because of ion-ion interactions. The model also predicts a crossover between different activation energy in the temperature dependent conductivity. When non-Debye relaxation is observed it is common to see such a change of activation energy, and the CM has been quite successful in describing such non-Arrhenius conductivity in several ionic conductors.[ 30 ] In this thesis, we used the CM to show that the change of activation energy observed around 50 ◦ C to 70 ◦ C in the temperature dependent conductivity of Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) could be explained by a single diffusion process involving significant ion-ion interactions (see Article II). A more detailed description of the model is given in the following Section 4 . 2 . 3 .

## 4 . 2 . 3 The Coupling Model

The Coupling Model was introduced by K. L. Ngai in 1979 .[ 31 ] Contrarily to the Debye model that considers the relaxation of identical, non-interacting dipoles , the CM assumes that in ionic conductors with a larger number of mobile charges or a disordered structure, interactions are bound to affect the relaxation after a certain time . This is the essence of the model that describes a cross-over between two relaxation regimes. At very short times, when interactions have not yet influenced the relaxation, the relaxation rate is a constant (like for the Debye model, see Eq. ( 4 . 14 )) but it becomes time dependent when particle interaction comes into play:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

For the correlation function this results in a cross-over between a Debye-type relaxtion at short times and a KWW-type relaxation at longer times:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

The time t c is temperature and material independent and near 1 ps. This value was later found to originate from classical chaos theory of non-linear systems.[ 32 ] The continuity between the two regimes then yields:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

which relates the interaction independent relaxation time t 0 to the cooperative or correlated relaxation time ts . Eq. ( 4 . 26 ) has an interesting effect on the temperature dependence of the ionic conductivity. Indeed, because the relaxation time t 0 is temperature activated but the cross-over time t c is assumed to be temperature independent, two regimes arise. If t 0 GLYPH&lt;181&gt; exp ( Ea / kb T ) if follows from Eq. ( 4 . 26 ) that ts GLYPH&lt;181&gt; exp ( Ea b / kb T ) . Then, at low temperature, when t 0 &gt;&gt; t c the simple exponential has decayed of only an insignificant amount at t = t c and the relaxation is dominated by the stretched exponential. On the contrary, at high temperature when t 0 &lt;&lt; t c ,the simple exponential dominates the relaxation. This is shown in Fig. 4 . 2 .

Figure 4 . 2 : Decay of the Coupling Model relaxation function at low and high temperature. The dotted line correspond to the exponential decay at t &lt; tc while the continuous lines represents the stretched exponential decay at t &gt; tc .

This cross-over in the dominating relaxation behavior is reflected in s dc through Eq. ( 4 . 19 ). At high temperatures, the temperature evolution of s dc follows that of t 0 with an activation energy Ea while at low temperature it is dominated by ts with an activation energy Ea / b . For some ionic conductors this cross-over can be observed at relatively low temperature. For example, a change of activation energy was observed in Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) around 50 ◦ C to 70 ◦ C and could be explained with the CM (see Article II).

The relaxation cross-over is also reflected in the frequency dependent conductivity and electric modulus as they are related to F ( w ) . Three regimes may be expected:

- At very high frequency, i. e. at timescale where interactions play no major role, a frequency independent conductivity can be expected. This regime is however often inaccessible to standard impedance spectroscopy apparatus as it requires THz frequency.
- Towards lower frequencies, a dependence of the conductivity on w is observed as a result from the dominating stretched exponential relaxation.
- Finally, at low enough frequencies the conductivity becomes frequency independent and reaches s dc as F ( t ) has fully relaxed at long timescales.

Importantly, the frequency dependent region can be studied to characterize the correlation, i. e. to extract the value of the b parameter relating t 0 and ts in Eq. ( 4 . 27 ). Indeed as a result of the CM, the frequency dependence of the real part of s ( w ) will follow a power law dependence related to b . Similarly, the imaginary part of the electric modulus will deviate from the ideal Lorentzian shape predicted by the Debye model and show an asymmetric shape. At low frequency corresponding to the s dc region, M ′′ ( w ) GLYPH&lt;181&gt; w while in the region where conductivity also show frequency dependence M ′′ ( w ) GLYPH&lt;181&gt; w -b . This behavior if illustrated in Fig. 4 . 3 .

Figure 4 . 3 : Expected behavior of the real part of the conductivity and imaginary part of the electric modulus as a function of frequency in the framework of the Coupling Model. Reproduced by permission from ref. [ 33 ], Elsevier, Electrochemica Acta, 2019 .

Cuervo-Reyes and al. have developed an analytical approximation to fit electric modulus data in the framework of the CM.[ 33 ] The

approximation allows to avoid the use of numerical Fourier transforms of F ( w ) which have no analytical solution in the case of Eq. ( 4 . 25 ).

The model fits M ( w ) with two functions at high and low frequencies, joined by a low-pass and high-pass filters:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

The exact definition of Mlow ( w ) and Mhigh ( w ) along with the code for the fitting of the model written for IgorPro are given in the Appendix.

This model was used in Article II to extract b from the fit of M ′′ ( w ) for Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ). b was found to be in agreement with the value extracted from the ratio between high and low temperature activation energy for the ionic conductivity, as predicted by the CM. In addition, the model allows to extract the high frequency dielectric constant # ¥ . The value found for Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) is consistent with the experimental value of the activation energy of the ionic conduction at high temperature, according to an empirical relation between the two.[ 34 ]

## 4 . 2 . 4 Microscopic interpretations and relation to the conductivity formalism

The CM only describes the macroscopic relaxation of an electric field in a conductor and lacks a microscopic interpretation in terms of the motion of charged particles. In most cases when studying an ionic conductor, one is interested to get such an insight. Different attempt have been made to give such interpretation, e. g. to explain the value of b in the stretched exponential relaxation.

J.C. Phillips has derived two magic numbers , i. e. special values of b for which a microscopic interpretation can be given.[ 35 ] These values are b = d d + 2 and b = d d + 4 where d is the dimensionality of the diffusion. The former arises when the moving charged particles undergo short range Coulomb interaction while the second involve more long range interactions. In Article II a b value of 0 . 6 was found for Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) which corresponds exactly to b = d d + 2 for a three dimensional diffusion expected in the cubic structure of Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ).

Monte Carlo simulations also showed the importance of Coulomb interactions to explain non-Arrhenius thermal activation of conductivity predicted by the CM.[ 36 ] However, Coulomb interactions alone are not sufficient to explain the cross-over of activation energy. The simulations also point out the importance of background fluctuation in the energy landscape. At low temperatures, when the thermal energy is low compared to these fluctuations they play an important role in slowing down the relaxation. A higher activation energy for s dc is then observed which then is reduced at higher temperatures when the fluc-

tuations are insignificant compared to the thermal energy. In Article II, librational motion of the anions were observed and interpreted as the origin of such fluctuations. Combining NMR measurements and neutron scattering, the intensity of these fluctuation was found to be in good agreement with the cross over temperature in the conductivity data.

Finally, it is interesting to relate the Coupling Model and the Jump Relaxation Model which also considers a slowing down of the relaxation after a certain time. In this model, interactions between mobile charges impose a non-zero probability for correlated forwardbackward jumps such that not all jumps can be successful at all times. Only at very short times, i. e. high frequencies, all jumps can be successful (there is not enough time for a backward jump) and the conductivity takes a constant value, significantly higher than that of s dc . Then at very long times only the successful jumps participate in the value of s dc . In the intermediate regimes, only the jumps that are successful within a time 1/ w participate in the conductivity and a frequency dependence is observed.

It can be shown that in this intermediate regime the mean square displacement shows a power law dependence with time, i. e. 〈 r 2 ( t ) 〉 GLYPH&lt;181&gt; t b while in the two other regimes 〈 r 2 ( t ) 〉 GLYPH&lt;181&gt; t . This means that the velocity correlation function is also time dependent and therefore the conductivity also shows frequency dependence (see Section 4 . 1 . 2 ). In fact the following relation holds relating back to the conductivity formalism:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

## 4 . 3 nmr spin lattice relaxation

As mentioned in Section 3 . 2 . 5 , the motion of atoms in a material can have a significant effect on the spin dynamics through different interactions. The study of this spin relaxation can thus be a powerful technique to collect details of ion dynamics in various classes of ionic conductors.[ 37 , 38 ] We know that at equilibrium the spins align with the B 0 field leading to Mz ( t ) = M 0. This distribution can be perturbed through appropriate radio frequency pulses causing the z-component of M ( t ) to vanish (e. g. 90 ◦ pulse). This non-equilibrium state is typically produced in a so-called saturation experiment. The recovery of Mz ( t ) = 0 toward M 0 then involves two types of relaxation shown in Fig. 4 . 4 : the decay of the magnetization in the x-y plane called spin-spin relaxation, and the recovery of the magnetization along the z-axis called spin-lattice relaxation. While both relaxation can yield information about atomic motion it is more common to study the latter in a NMR spin-lattice relaxation (SLR) experiment.

Commonly,the recovery of Mz ( t ) = 0 toward M 0 follows an exponential function according to:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where T 1 is the characteristic relaxation time. T 1 depends on both the Larmor frequency w 0 and temperature.

Figure 4 . 4 : Illustration of the relaxation of the magnetization in the x-y plane, drive by spin-spin relation and along the z-axis, driven by spin lattice relaxation.

At temperatures where diffusive motion causes magnetic field fluctuations on the timescale of the Larmor frequency (generally near room temperature for fast ion conductors), the recovery of Mz ( t ) will be mainly induced by such diffusive motion. The fluctuations can originate from the spin-carrying atoms/ion under investigation or sensed by the ion if neighboring species are involved in rapid motional processes (e. g. through dipolar coupling). There is thus a direct link between between T 1 and the motion of ions. Similarly to the electric modulus and conductivity formalism, this linked can be formalized with the help of a relaxation or correlation function defined as:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where D w ( t ) are the time fluctuation of the spin precession frequency caused by interactions. G ( 0 ) is a measure of the interaction strength of the spin with the local field, while g ( t ) is a normalized relaxation function like F ( t ) in Eq. ( 4 . 1 ).

The relaxation rate R 1 = 1/ T 1 is then related to the Spectral Density Function J ( w ) which is the Fourier transform of such a relaxation function. In 1948 Bloembergen, Purcell and Pound (BPP), developed a model to describe spin relaxation influenced by random jump diffusion in three dimensions.[ 39 ] In the BPP model, the relaxation function of

the fluctuating magnetic dipole interactions is described by a simple decaying exponential, analogously to the Debye dipole relaxation:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

In a NMR experiment, the frequency is fixed and cannot be varied over several orders of magnitude as in impedance spectroscopy. Therefore, information about different timescales of diffusion motion is accessed by varying the temperature. The relaxation time carries the temperature dependence of the diffusion motion and t c = t c ,0 exp ( Ea / kb T ) . R 1 ( T ) then shows a characteristic temperature dependence. A low temperature, i. e. in the limit w 0 t c &gt;&gt; 1, R 1 increases with temperature with an activation energy Ea . When w 0 t c glyph[similarequal] 1 the coupling between the precessing spins and the fluctuating field is the most efficient and the relaxation rate reaches a maximum. The temperature of the peak maximum is thus related to the timescale of the diffusive motion giving rise to the spin relaxation. Then, as the ion dynamics gets faster at higher temperatures, the relaxation rate decreases again with an identical activation energy of Ea in the limit w 0 t c &lt;&lt; 1.

Similarly to the case of Debye model, the simple BPP case is rarely observed experimentally for fast ion conductors in which mobile ions do not diffuse with a random 3 D motion. As for the conductivity relaxation it means that the decay of the relaxation function tend to follow a KWW type of behavior, i. e. g ( t ) = exp ( -[ | t | / t c ] b ) , with 0 &lt; b ≤ 1.

As a result, the shape of R 1 ( T ) is modified. The slope of the low temperature flank of the rate peak will change and the activation energy is reduced by a factor b while the activation energy of the high temperature remains unchanged. We then have:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

This is in contrast with the effect of a KWW type relaxation (or from the Coupling Model) on the temperature dependence of the conductivity where the low temperature activation energy is increased by a factor 1/ b . The reason is that when plotting the conductivity, one always looks at s dc , i. e. long range diffusion motion at rather long timescales. In contrast, in NMR the timescale is fixed by the magnetic field intensity. Therefore, when varying the temperature different types of motion are probed. Long range motions are probed at high temperature when ions can diffuse far enough within the fixed timescale. In contrast, at low temperature more local motion are sensed.

If we consider that a KWW type of relaxation is caused by ion-ion interactions, the result of Eq. ( 4 . 32 ) can be understood as follow. At low temperature, the interactions do not yet affect the ions which are

confined in a local motion. The interactions only play a role for long range diffusion thereby increasing the activation energy.

## 4 . 3 . 1 Relation and differences between NMR and conductivity relaxation

It seems clear from the equation derived in the previous sections that there are similarities between the laws which describe the relaxation of spins and that of the electric field caused by moving charges. The relation between the different relaxation function and the experimental data are shown in Table 4 . 1 :

Table 4 . 1 : Notation for the different functions used to describe conductivity and NMR spin lattice relaxation.

|                                              | Conductivity relax- ation          | Spin-lattice relaxa- tion                |
|----------------------------------------------|------------------------------------|------------------------------------------|
| Relaxation function                          | F ( t )                            | g ( t )                                  |
| Fourier transform of the relaxation function | M ( w ) , s ( w )                  | J ( w )                                  |
| Activation energies                          | E lowT a , s = E highT a , s / b s | E lowT a , NMR = b NMR · E highT a , NMR |

However, while the relaxation functions follow similar mathematics, they derive from different physical phenomenons and cannot be strictly compared quantitatively.

Still, qualitative comparison can help to better understand the underlying mechanisms governing ion diffusion in a potential solid electrolyte. Both NMR and impedance spectroscopy can be used to extract activation energies for long range diffusion. Then, the activation energy of the high temperature flank of the R 1 ( T ) peak can be compared to that of Ea , s dc . This comparison should be done within the same temperature range, particularly in the case where ion-ion correlations are present. The low temperature flank activation energy may be compared with that of the s ac at high frequencies which also probes local motions. However this is rarely done for ionic conductors where long range diffusion is ultimately more relevant.

In addition, multiple NMR rate peaks can arise due to different diffusion processes present within the same material. Because at the peak maximum the relation w 0 t c glyph[similarequal] 1 holds, the diffusion timescale can be extracted and compared to that found with conductivity. This can for example allow to identify which process is dominating the longrange diffusion sensed with impedance spectroscopy. In Article II, two relaxation processes where observed in NMR. From the comparison of the timescale of the diffusion process as well as the activation energies,

the faster process observed at lower temperature in NMR was found to be dominating the behavior observed in conductivity.

NMR can also allow to distinguish and/or relate the relaxation behavior of different atomic species in a material. For example, in Article II, it was found that the main relaxation rate peak for 23 Na, 11 B and 1 H all reached a maximum near the same temperature. In addition, activation energies and b were found to be very similar for these three atoms. This means that the relaxation of their respective spins is caused by the same diffusive motion and thus that the motion of the cations (Na + ) and anions (containing B and H) is linked. This allowed to identify cation-anion interactions and link them to the observed non-Arrhenius temperature dependence of the conductivity. Indeed the b = 0.6 value indicate short range Coulomb interaction in line with such cation-anion interactions.

Finally, it is important to note that the NMR correlation function involves spin interaction which are strongly dependent on the orientation. Therefore, the spin relaxation is sensitive to the dimension of the atomic motion. While for 3 D motion the high temperature flank of R 1 ( T ) is frequency independent this is not the case for motions confined to lower dimensions. This can be probed also by doing the same relaxation measurements in two NMR spectrometers with a different magnet strength. Alternatively, one can perform measurements in the so-called rotating frame of reference. This is done by applying a constant RF field called a locking field. The effect of the field, much like that of a RF pulse is to change the effective magnetic field felt by the spins. In general, locking field in the kHz in contrast to MHz for standard NMR fields. This significant change of frequency also allows to better probe much slower diffusion processes. In Article II NMR-SLR measurements at different frequencies (including spin-lock SLR) allowed to show that the main diffusion process responsible for s dc was three dimensional, while a second slower process was caused by a more confined motion.

- [ 34 ] K. Wakamura. 'Effects of electronic band on activation energy and of effective charge on lattice distortion in superionic conductors.' In: Journal of Physics and Chemistry of Solids 59 . 5 ( 1998 ), pp. 591 -598 . doi : 10.1016/S0022-3697(97)00247-3 .

## Part III

## PUBLICATIONS

## SUMMARY OF THE PAPERS

## 5 . 1 article i &amp; ii

Synthesis and characterization of a new hydro-closo-borate solid electrolyte

Article I presents the synthesis and characterization of Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) in view of its use as a solid electrolyte for all-solid-state batteries.

Na 2 B 12 H 12 and Na 2 B 10 H 10 both undergo structural phase transitions at 260 ◦ C and 100 ◦ C respectively, which trigger high Na + conductivity. These transition are however reversible and the high ionic conductivity is not maintained at room temperature. This work shows that mechanical milling of Na 2 B 12 H 12 and Na 2 B 10 H 10 followed by a heat treatment yield a new single phase material, namely Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ). This materials exhibits a high room temperature Na + conductivity, several orders of magnitude higher than that of the two precursors materials. X-ray diffraction shows that the crystal symmetry of Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) at 30 ◦ C is the same as is Na 2 B 10 H 10 above 100 ◦ C, i. e. the anions show a face-centered cubic arrangement. Thermal characterization confirms that Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) undergoes no further structural phase transition in a large temperature range in contrast to Na 2 B 12 H 12 and Na 2 B 10 H 10 .

In contrast to Na 2 B 10 H 10 , Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) contains two anions in its structure, whose presence in confirmed by infrared and NMR spectroscopy. These techniques also show that the anions exhibit a fast dynamic. As a result Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) possesses a high Na + conductivity of 0.9 mS cm -1 at 20 ◦ C, comparable to that of a standard liquid electrolyte and high enough to use Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) as a solid electrolyte in a ASSBs.

To confirm the potential of Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) as a solid electrolyte its electrochemical stability window is accurately evaluated using linear sweep voltammetry. The material is found to be stable in contact with sodium metal and is not oxidized before 3 V vs. Na/Na + . Above this voltage, small oxidation currents on the nA cm -2 range only were measured. The stability towards sodium metal is then further confirmed in a galvanostatic stripping plating experiment which constitute a first step towards device integration.

This article is reproduced below in Article I and published in:

- [ 1 ] L. Duchêne, R.-S. Kühnel, D. Rentsch, A. Remhof, H. Hagemann, and C. Battaglia. 'A highly stable sodium solid-state electrolyte based on a dodeca/deca-borate equimolar mixture.' In: Chemical Communications 53 . 30 ( 2017 ), pp. 4195 -4198 . doi : 10.1039/c7cc00794a .

## Comments on my contribution

I performed the synthesis of Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) and most of the materials characterization (DSC, IS, XRD, IR).

Dr. Daniel Rentsch carried out the NMR experiments and also helped with the analysis of the data. Infrared spectroscopy was performed at the University of Geneva with the help of Prof. Hans Hagemann.

Together with Dr. Ruben-Simon Kühnel, we chose the experimental conditions for the electrochemical characterization (voltammetry and galvanostatic cyclic).

Finally, I wrote the manuscript with inputs from all the co-authors.

Article II focuses on more fundamental aspects of the ionic conduction mechanism in Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ). In Article I it was observed that this materials exhibits a nonstandard temperature dependence of the ionic conductivity and shows an activation energy that varies with temperature. Here we show that three distinct regimes can be identified and give a detailed explanation for the conduction mechanisms in each of them.

Impedance spectroscopy first confirms that only one contribution is responsible for the ionic conductivity of Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ), i. e. that the presence of grain boundaries cannot explain the change of activation energy. Instead, a glass transition identified by XRD and DSC at -50 ◦ C causes a change of sodium ordering in the structure which can explain the first change of the activation energy at that temperature.

The ionic conduction mechanism then involves significant ion-ion interactions which cause another change of the activation energy around 70 ◦ C. This behavior originates from a frequency dependence of the ionic conductivity as described in Chapter 4 . Fitting of the electric modulus and ionic conductivity based on the Coupling Model confirms that the non-Arrhenius behavior at higher temperature can be fully explained by a single contribution to the ionic conductivity that involved interactions between moving charges.

These interactions are also observed in NMR relaxation measurements which show a link between cation and anion dynamics. The activation energies extracted from the NMR results are in agreement with the high temperature activation energy seen in conductivity

which thereby reflects the microscopic energy barrier for sodium diffusion.

Neutron scattering results bring further insight into the geometry of the anion motion which perform two types of motions. Jump rotations around the anions' symmetry axis are found to have an activation energy similar to that found with NMR and are linked to long-range sodium diffusion. This result confirms the link between cation and anion motions. Then, librational motion are identified and linked to background energy fluctuations also observed in NMR at low temperature. These fluctuations in the energy landscape are sensed by the diffusing cation only at low temperature. They thus provide an explanation for the transition temperature at 70 ◦ C above which ion-ion correlations play a less significant role.

Finally, we discuss the link between the observed behavior and the different structural and dynamical frustrations inherent to ionic diffusion in closo -borate materials. We show that our interpretation also applies to related compounds and provides a way to tune the properties of these electrolytes.

This article is reproduced below with permission in Article II. It is published in:

- [ 2 ] L. Duchêne, S. Lunghammer, T. Burankova, W.-C. Liao, J. P. Embs, C. Copéret, H. M. R. Wilkening, A. Remhof, H. Hagemann, and C. Battaglia. 'Ionic Conduction Mechanism in the Na 2 (B 12 H 12 ) 0 . 5 (B 10 H 10 ) 0 . 5 closo -Borate Solid-State Electrolyte: Interplay of Disorder and Ion-Ion Interactions.' In: Chemistry of Materials 31 . 9 ( 2019 ), pp. 3449 -3460 . doi : 10.1021/acs.chemmater. 9b00610 .

## Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society

## 5 . 2 article iii &amp; iv

Processing, device integration and device performance of all-solid-state batteries using the Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) solid electrolyte

Article III describes the integration of Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) in an allsolid-state battery. Following the electrochemical characterizations in Article I, NaCrO 2 is chosen as a cathode material because its main electrochemical activity occurs near 3 V, thereby maximizing the use of the stability window of the solid electrolyte. Similarly, sodium metal is chosen as anode material, also because of its potential for high energy devices. The sodium metal anode however restricts the cycling of the battery to 60 ◦ C to avoid issues of inhomogeneous plating observed at room temperature.

The paper focuses mainly on the effect of the cathode composite preparation on the cycling stability of the cell. The cathode composite contains the active material, here NaCrO 2 , that is mixed with conductive carbon and the solid electrolyte to make both electrons and sodium ions available for the electrochemical cycling of the battery. Hand mixing of these three components yield rather poor cycling stability of the solid-state cells. Coating part of the electrolyte onto the cathode particle via a solution based impregnation method significantly improves the cycling stability of the cell. After 250 charge-discharge cycles at a rate of C/ 5 the cell retains 85 % of its initial cycling stability. The contact improvement between cathode and electrolyte is visible by electron microscopy, and further confirmed by the time evolution of the cell impedance which does not increase significantly upon cycling for the impregnated cathode, contrarily to the hand-mixed one.

Finally, the more important capacity fading of the cell when cycled to higher voltages serves as a confirmation to the careful evaluation of the ESW in Article I

This article is reproduced below in Article III and published in:

- [ 3 ] L. Duchêne, R.-S. Kühnel, E. Stilp, E. C. Reyes, A. Remhof, H. Hagemann, and C. Battaglia. 'A stable 3 Vall-solid-state sodiumion battery based on a closo -borate electrolyte.' In: Energy &amp;

Environmental Science 10 . 12 ( 2017 ), pp. 2609 -2615 . doi : 10.1039/ c7ee02420g .

## Comments on my contribution

I synthesized all the materials, prepared the electrodes and assembled the cells. Dr. Ruben-Simon Kühnel helped to design the experiments for the electrochemical cell tests.

Dr. Evelyn Stilp acquired the SEM images on samples prepared by me.

I measured electrochemical impedance spectroscopy and analyzed the results with the help of Dr. Eduardo Cuervo-Reyes.

Finally, I wrote the manuscript with inputs from all the co-authors.

Article IV builds on the result obtained in Article III, notably on the solution processing of the solid electrolyte. First, isopropanol is identified as a suitable solvent to crystallize Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) with the appropriate crystal structure and ionic conductivity (methanol used in Article III does not yield in the crystallization of the appropriate phase). This result shows that the solid electrolyte can be obtained entirely from solution avoiding any mechanical milling process used in previous works.

This solution processability is used to infiltrate the solid electrolyte into electrodes fabricated with a conventional slurry process already established for current battery technologies. Electrolyte infiltrated NaCrO 2 cathodes and Sn anodes are fabricated with this process. In comparison to Article III the mass loading of the electrode is significantly increased. In order to avoid problems of inhomogeneous sodium metal plating when cycling with high currents a Na-Sn alloy is used as the counter electrode in place of pure sodium metal.

Infiltrated NaCrO 2 cathodes and Sn anodes are tested in half cells at room temperature and show remarkable cycling stability. Particularly, half cells using NaCrO 2 cathodes can retain 95 . 6 % of their initial capacity after 100 cycles. This capacity retention is obtained by applying a pressure of 70 MPa but is only reduced to 85 . 9 % when the pressure is decreased to 3 MPa. This confirms the intimated electrode-electrolyte obtained with the solution process. Rate tests show an excellent capacity retention up to 5 C and current density of 4.5 mA cm -2 .

Finally, by matching the capacity of a NaCrO 2 cathode and Sn anode, the performance of a first all-solid-state rocking-chair full cell using a closo -borate solid electrolyte is evaluated.

This article is submitted and currently under review.

Then, this work was carried out during a three month exchange I did in the laboratory of Prof. Yoon Seok Jung, in Hanyang University, South Korea, in the framework of the Young Researcher Exchange Program between Switzerland &amp; Korea. ## 5 . 3 article v

This invited review presents the recent achievements on hydroborate solid electrolytes with an emphasis on intrinsic materials properties and on aspects related to their integration into devices. In particular the main metrics for solid electrolytes are evaluated and compared to those of other major class of solid electrolytes.

The review focuses on historical development of hydroborate electrolytes to achieve high room temperature conductivity. An emphasis is made on the unique behavior of these materials in terms of ion dynamics compared to other well studied families of solid electrolytes.

The practical challenges for the integration of hydroborates in allsolid-state batteries are then summarized. The known results on thermal and electrochemical stability of these compounds as well as compatibility issues with potential electrode materials are commented to identify remaining research gaps. A focus is put on the most promising materials within the hydroborate family i. e. closo -borates and closo -carba-borates.

Then, the results obtained for the most promising sodium and lithium based devices using such a hydroborate solid electrolyte are given and evaluated in the perspective of the strong promises held for all-solid-state batteries.

Finally, routes to establish potentially cheaper synthesis methods for closo -borates and closo -carba-borates are asserted.

This review was submitted following an invitation to contribute to an upcoming special issue in solid-state battery in the journal Energy Storage Materials. It is published in:

- [ 4 ] L. Duchêne, A. Remhof, H. Hagemann, and C. Battaglia. 'Status and prospects of hydroborate electrolytes for all-solid-state batteries.' In: Energy Storage Materials ( 2019 ). doi : 10.1016/j.ensm. 2019.08.032 .

## Comments on my contribution

I performed the literature survey, prepared the figures and wrote the majority of the manuscript with inputs from all the co-authors. Prof. Hans Hagemann wrote the section regarding synthesis methods and the abstract was written by Dr. Corsin Battaglia.

ARTICLE I

6

## ChemComm

## COMMUNICATION

Cite this: Chem. Commun., 2017, 53 , 4195

Received 30th January 2017, Accepted 22nd March 2017

DOI: 10.1039/c7cc00794a

rsc.li/chemcomm

Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5, a new solid-state sodium electrolyte is shown to offer high Na + conductivity of 0.9 mS cm /C0 1 at 20 8 C, excellent thermal stability up to 300 8 C, and a large electrochemical stability window of 3 V including stability towards sodium metal anodes, all essential prerequisites for a stable room-temperature 3 V all-solid-state sodium-ion battery.

All-solid-state sodium-ion batteries promise to simultaneously yield better performance and lower cost as compared to state-ofthe-art lithium-ion technology using organic liquid electrolytes. 1,2 To build such a competitive all-solid-state battery requires a solidstate electrolyte (SSE) with high ionic conductivity near room temperature and high thermal and electrochemical stability. Meeting these three requirements simultaneously still represents a major challenge. High ionic conductivity of or above 10 /C0 3 S cm /C0 1 has been reached in oxide ceramics or more recently in sulfide phases but these materials still suffer from high electrodeelectrolyte interface resistance or intrinsically small electrochemical stability windows of 1 V or less, respectively. 3-10 Recently, complex hydride materials and particularly metal closo -borates have emerged as promising alternative SSEs. 11,12 Metal closo -borates are salts with polyhedral B n H n 2 /C0 ( n = 6-12) anions, among which the salts of the B10H10 2 /C0 and B12H12 2 /C0 anions were early noted due to their high thermal and chemical stability. 13 LiCB11H12 as a halogen free electrolyte was also predicted by theory 14 and used in experiments to synthesize a Mg-electrolyte. 15 closo -Borate and related carborate salts (with monovalent CB n /C0 1H n /C0 anions) also exhibit structural transitions to highly conductive phases, above room temperature. 16-19 Strategies to stabilize these superionic phases at room temperature and below, including mechanical milling and cation or anion substitutions, have been implemented. 20-23

b De ´partement de Chimie-Physique, Universite ´ de Gene `ve, CH-1211 Geneva 4, Switzerland

## A highly stable sodium solid-state electrolyte based on a dodeca/deca-borate equimolar mixture †

L. Duche

ˆne, ab R.-S. Ku ¨ hnel, a D. Rentsch, a A. Remhof, * a H. Hagemann b and a

C. Battaglia

Yet, studies on closo -borates have focused almost exclusively on improving conductivity, while only preliminary work has been performed on the assessment of their electrochemical stability window and of their suitability for integration into a battery. Here, we report a new sodium superionic conductor Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 with a conductivity close to 10 /C0 3 S cm /C0 1 at room temperature (20 1 C). We further demonstrate that this material is compatible with a sodium metal anode and fulfils all prerequisites to be used in a 3 V all-solid-state sodium-ion battery.

Fig. 1a shows differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) data for the individual precursors Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 and their reacted 1 : 1 equimolar mixture. Both precursors show a characteristic endothermic peak at the temperature of their respective structural phase transition, as previously described. 24,25 No such event is observed for the 1:1 mixture indicating that Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 does not undergo a phase transition within this temperature window. Besides the first heating cycle, we also show DSC data of the third heating cycle, which almost coincides with the first cycle, and demonstrates the stability of the equimolar

Fig. 1 (a) DSC data for pure Na2B12H12, Na2B10H10 and their equimolar 1 : 1 mixture, Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5. (b) Temperature-dependent Na + conductivity for pure Na2B12H12, Na2B10H10 16,17 and Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5. Empty circles and filled triangles shown for the 1:1 mixture are measurement points upon heating and cooling, respectively.

mixture when subjected to thermal cycling. Thermal characterization was also carried out for intermediate Na2(B12H12)1 /C0 x (B10H10) x compositions (0.1 o x o 0.6, see Fig. S1, ESI † ) for which the endothermic peaks are still visible but decrease in size and ultimately vanish as the ratio approaches x = 0.5, at which the precursors fully react.

Fig. 1b presents the ionic conductivity of Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 determined by impedance spectroscopy as a function of temperature (see Experimental section and Fig. S2, ESI, † for details). The conductivities of Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 obtained by Udovic et al. 16,17 are also reported for comparison. The room temperature ionic conductivity for the equimolar ratio is several orders of magnitude higher than that of Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10, reaching 0.9 mS cm /C0 1 at 20 1 C. This value is comparable to typical conductivities reported for oxide and sulfide electrolytes 3-8 and only slightly lower than that of a standard sodium liquid electrolyte based on 1 M NaClO4 dissolved in EC:PC (3.3 mS cm /C0 1 at 25 1 C, 26 assuming a sodium transference number of 0.4). Within the family of complex hydrides, this conductivity is comparable to the recently reported Na3BH4B12H12 and highly conducting, ballmilled Na2B12H12 21,22 and is only surpassed by equimolar mixed carborate phases that can reach exceptional conductivity of 70 mS cm /C0 1 at room temperature 23 but have yet to demonstrate their applicability as SSEs in all-solid-state batteries. Similarly to the observed thermal behavior, the equimolar mixture shows no signs of a phase transition in the temperature-dependent conductivity data, whilst the conductivity of the precursors drops sharply at the onset temperatures at which latent heat is observed in DSC. Consequently, while hysteresis in conductivity is observed for both precursors upon temperature cycling, the conductivity of the equimolar mixture shows no hysteresis between heating and cooling. Still, the activation energy for Na + diffusion in Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 decreases smoothly from 0.7 at /C0 40 1 C to 0.4 eV at 100 1 C with increasing temperature. A similar behavior was observed for carborate mixtures 23 and Na3BH4B12H12 21 and attributed to temperature dependent anion dynamics within the respective crystal structures. The connection between lattice parameter, anion dynamics and the evolution of cation conductivity in closo -borates was also recently discussed by Varley et al. based on ab initio molecular dynamics calculations. 27

Crystal structure and lattice parameters were determined by X-ray diffraction (XRD). In Fig. 2a we compare the diffractograms of the equimolar mixture at room temperature with the ones of the precursors in their respective high temperature (HT) conductive phases, i.e. body-centered cubic (bcc) Na2B12H12 at 300 1 C and face-centered cubic (fcc) Na2B10H10 at 180 1 C. At room temperature the precursors show additional reflections indicative of the lower structure symmetry below the phase transition (see Fig. S3a, ESI † ). We find that the reflections in the XRD pattern of the mixed phase can all be matched to the one of Na2B10H10 in its HT fcc phase. We conclude that the equimolar mixture stabilizes a single phase, isostructural to the highly conducting HT phase of Na2B10H10. Refinement yields a slightly larger unit cell parameter of a = 10.029 Å for Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 at room temperature vs. a = 9.854 Å for Na2B10H10 at 180 1 C (see Fig. S3b and c, ESI † ). Additionally, XRD

Fig. 2 (a) XRD patterns and (b) infrared spectra of the HT phases of Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 recorded at 300 and 180 1 C, respectively, compared to the room temperature phase of Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5. Characteristic features are marked by symbols. (c-e) Room temperature 1 H, 11 B and 23 Na MAS solid-state NMR spectra of Na2B12H12, Na2B10H10 and Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5. For the 1 H and 11 B NMR data, the Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 spectra were Y-offset and up-scaled by a factor of 10 to facilitate data comparison.

measurements of Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 at 300 1 C (see Fig. S3a, ESI † ) show no structural change other than an expansion of the unit cell parameters, demonstrating again the high thermal stability in agreement with DSC and conductivity measurements. Note that equimolar mixtures of Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 were recently reported by Tang et al. , 22 who also used mechanical milling followed by a heat treatment at 275 1 C for 16 h. However, their mixture still contained predominantly ( 4 70%) the low temperature (LT) phase of Na2B12H12 as refined from XRD, indicating only partial conversion to the mixed phase, and no conductivity measurement was reported. Our results prove that using appropriate reaction conditions, equimolar mixtures of Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 fully react and stabilize a single mixedanion phase.

To gain insight into the local anion configuration in the mixed-anion structure, we performed infrared (IR) spectroscopy measurements. Fig. 2b compares the IR spectra of the HT phases of both Na2B12H12 (300 1 C) and Na2B10H10 (180 1 C) with Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 at room temperature. For both precursors, B-H stretching and cage absorption modes are identified in the wavenumber ranges of 2400-2500 cm /C0 1 and 600-1200 cm /C0 1 , respectively. These characteristic absorption modes correspond to those previously assigned in the IR spectra of B10H10 2 /C0 and B12H12 2 /C0 measured in solution, 13,28 characteristic of the respective D 4d and I h point group symmetry of these ions. Recent theoretical DFT calculations confirm these assignments. 29 In the LT phases of Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 (see Fig. S4, ESI † ) significant splitting of the absorption modes can be seen, indicative of a lowered symmetry of the ions, whereas the anions in the HT phases adopt a similar symmetry than in the solution. Therefore, we conclude that they sit in a very symmetric environment that is a consequence

of the cubic symmetry of the crystal structure and of the absence of a preferred orientation of the anions, i.e. of rotational disorder. The IR spectrum of Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 at room temperature corresponds closely to the sum of the spectra of Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 in their respective HT phases and all vibration modes can be assigned to the two closo -borate anions. This shows that both B10H10 2 /C0 and B12H12 2 /C0 are still present in the structure and suggests either an average of rotationally disordered but static configurations, or of dynamic rotation of the anions.

To distinguish between these two cases, we recorded solidstate 11 B, 1 H and 23 Na magic-angle spinning nuclear magnetic resonance (MAS NMR) spectra of Na2B12H12, Na2B10H10 and their 1 : 1 mixture. All samples were measured at room temperature to compare the ion dynamics. As seen from comparison in Fig. 2c-e, the spectrum of the equimolar mixture comprises all the resonances of the constituents in the precursors. Importantly, the resonances of the equimolar mixture are all considerably narrowed compared to the signals of the pure precursors, especially in the case of boron and hydrogen (Fig. 2c-e). This narrowing is a sign of fast dynamics of the ions in the 1 : 1 mixture compared to the borate starting materials. We may thus also interpret the IR data as a result of fast anion dynamics rather than an average of frozen configurations. Additionally, since X-ray diffraction already showed that Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 has a well-defined crystal structure, we can conclude that this motion is rotational and not translational (see Fig. 3), as previously suggested based on NMR and quasi-elastic neutron scattering experiments on other closo -borates. 30,31 11 B NMR also confirms that the B12H12 2 /C0 and B10H10 2 /C0 anions remain intact in Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 with no significant 11 B signal intensity detectable other than the ones of the closo -borate anions. The 1 : 1 B12H12 2 /C0 to B10H10 2 /C0 ratio was verified by 11 B solution-state NMR by dissolving Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 in deuterated water, and it was confirmed that both anions are indeed present in a 1 : 1 molar ratio (see Fig. S5, ESI † ). Finally, it can be seen in Fig. 2e that the 23 Na NMR chemical shift and thus the sodium environment in Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 is averaged between that of Na2B10H10

Fig. 3 Simplified structure of Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 based on the HT phase of Na2B10H10. Partially occupied Na + ions sites are shown in different colors to distinguish tetrahedral and octahedral coordination. B12H12 2 /C0 and B10H10 2 /C0 anions are randomly distributed in the fcc framework. Two example anions are shown.

and Na2B12H12, supporting the hypothesis of a solid solution phase, as depicted in Fig. 3.

To evaluate the suitability of Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 as SSE in an all-solid-state sodium-ion battery, we performed cyclic voltammetry tests at room temperature. Fig. 4a shows current density vs. voltage measured for a Na|Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5|Pt cell cycled at voltages between /C0 0.5 V and 6.5 V at 0.1 mV s /C0 1 . Near 0 V, we observed characteristic anodic and cathodic current peaks associated with sodium stripping and plating. Towards higher voltages, the current density remains low up to 6.5 V but first oxidation currents were observed in the nA cm /C0 2 range. We therefore built two additional cells for a precise measure of the electrochemical stability window of Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5. To determine its oxidative stability, a platinum working electrode (WE) was swept against a sodium counter electrode from the open-circuit voltage of 1.6 V up to a voltage of 5.5 V at a slow rate of 0.1 mV s /C0 1 to remain near equilibrium. Results are shown in Fig. 4b. Small currents indicative of slow electrolyte oxidation can be measured for cell voltages above 3 V but remain below 10 nA cm /C0 2 at higher voltages. To determine the reductive stability, an aluminum working electrode, which unlike platinum does not alloy with sodium, was preferred. Starting at the cell's open circuit voltage of 1.6 V, the voltage was swept down to /C0 0.5 V. On aluminum, no reduction currents are observed down to 0 V, below which the sodium plating peak appears, suggesting that the electrolyte is stable in contact with metallic sodium.

We conclude that Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 offers a practical electrochemical stability window of 3 V, most likely limited by

Fig. 4 Room temperature cyclic voltammogram of (a) a Na|Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10 H10)0.5|Pt cell from /C0 0.5 to 6.5 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s /C0 1 and (b) of Na|Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 |Al &amp; Na|Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5|Pt cells between /C0 0.5 and 5.5 V showing the oxidative (red) and reductive (black) stability of the electrolyte, respectively. (c) Galvanostatic cycling of a symmetric Na|Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5|Na cell at 60 1 C with a current density of 100 m A cm /C0 2 .

|

oxidation of the B10H10 2 /C0 anions. 32 Preliminary cyclic voltammetry measurements performed on other closo -borate electrolytes suggested a much larger stability window for this class of compounds. 17,21,22 However, we believe that the stability has been over-estimated due to the very small oxidation currents that may not have been detected in previous measurements. In fact, Han et al. 33 recently discussed the limitation of conventional cyclic voltammetry measurements to evaluate the true stability window of solid-state electrolytes. This limitation can be overcome only if one is able to measure very small currents (we used a low current channel with pA resolution) or by artificially increasing the contact area between the electrolyte and the working electrode.

To further confirm the compatibility with a sodium metal anode, we built a symmetric Na|Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5|Na cell which was galvanostatically cycled at 60 1 C. Fig. 4c shows the evolution of the cell voltage over time as sodium is plated and stripped at a current of /C6 100 m A cm /C0 2 in intervals of 12 h. Our results demonstrate that sodium is plated and stripped fully reversibly with a low combined over-potential reaching a maximum of 1.2 mV, in good agreement with the measured conductivity for the electrolyte at 60 1 C. Importantly, the overall voltage remains stable for 12 days confirming that the electrolyte is not degrading. Note that during a 12 h cycle, a charge equivalent of 1.2 mA h cm /C0 2 is cycled through the material. Considering a typical sodium cathode material with a capacity of 120 mA h g /C0 1 and a mass loading of 20 mg, each 12 h cycle of this measurement corresponds to 50% of a full charge.

In conclusion, we demonstrated that among SSEs with comparable conductivity, Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 stands out as a particularly stable material, notably when compared to sulphide phases. The electrochemical stability window of 3 V is compatible with high performance sodium cathode materials such as Na2FePO4F, NaCrO2. We further demonstrated that Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 is compatible with a sodium metal anode enabling long-term and reversible sodium plating and stripping. We also believe that further anion engineering e.g. by mixing larger halogenated closo -borates with a lower charge density, could yield even higher stability and conductivity and that this class of materials holds promise for competitive high voltage all-solid-state sodium-ion batteries.

## Supplementary Information

## A highly stable sodium solid-state electrolyte based on a dodeca/deca-borate equimolar mixture

Léo Duchêne a,b , Ruben-Simon Kühnel a , Daniel Rentsch a , Arndt Remhof  *a , Hans Hagemann b , Corsin Battaglia a a Empa, Swiss Federal Laboratories for Materials Science and Technology, CH-8600 Dübendorf, Switzerland. b Département de Chimie-Physique, Université de Genève, CH-1211 Geneva 4, Switzerland.

*Email : arndt.remhof@empa.ch

## Experimental Details

Synthesis :  Na2B12H12  (anhydrous) and Na2B10H10  were purchased from Katchem. Na2B12H12 was used and characterized as received and Na2B10H10 was first dried under vacuum for 6 h at 160 °C. Na2(B12H12)1-x(B10H10)x samples with composition x=0.1 to 0.6 were prepared by mixing the appropriate amounts of precursors using mechanical ball-milling. Samples were ballmilled in a Spex 8000M shaker mill with 5 mm balls and a balls-to-sample mass ratio of 10 to 1 for three times 15 min with 5 min breaks to avoid overheating (sample referred to as 'before heat treatment'). After ball-milling, a heat treatment was carried out under vacuum (p &lt; 10 -3  mbar) at 270 °C for 12 h to fully react and crystalize the compounds (sample referred to as 'after heat treatment'). Samples were handled in an argon-filled glove-box to avoid exposure to moisture.

Differential Scanning Calorimetry (DSC) : Measurements were conducted on a Netzsch STA449 F3 Jupiter. The samples were cycled three times at heating and cooling rates of 10 K min -1  between -100 °C and 300 °C. The samples (~10 mg) were handled in sealed Al crucibles and the measurements were carried out under He flow.

Powder X-ray Diffraction (XRD) : XRD patterns were collected on a Bruker D8 diffractometer equipped with a Göebel mirror selecting  CuK α radiaton (λ=1.5418 Å).   Samples were sealed into quartz capillaries,  filled  under  argon  atmosphere.  High temperature measurements were performed using an MRI high temperature capillary furnace.

The refinements were performed using the Maud refinement software ( L. Lutterotti, S. Matthies, H.-R. Wenk, A.J. Schultz and J. Richardson, J. Appl. Phys., 81[2], 594-600, 1997 ). The background was interpolated from manually chosen points. As there is no structural model for Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5, we used the structural model of Na2B10H10 (Udovic et al. 17 ) to refine the lattice parameters only.

Structure visualizations were obtained in VESTA ( K. Momma and F. Izumi,  J. Appl. Crystallogr., 44, 1272-1276, 2011 )

NMR spectroscopy : Solid-state magic angle spinning (MAS) NMR measurements experiments were performed on a Bruker Avance-400 NMR spectrometer using a 4 mm CP-MAS probe. The  1 H,  11 B and  23 Na NMR spectra were recorded at 13 kHz MAS rates as single pulse experiments using pulse lengths &lt; π/6 applying SPINAL-64 proton decoupling in the case of boron (no influence observed on line width for  23 Na NMR spectra with or without decoupling of  1 H). Sufficiently long relaxation delays for full recovery of signal intensities were applied.

Infrared  spectroscopy :  IR  spectra  were  acquired  on  a  temperature  controlled  Specac  ATR  setup  mounted  on  a  Biorad Excalibur FT-IR instrument and measured between 600 and 4000 cm -1  with a resolution of 1 cm -1 .

AC and DC measurements : The powder samples were pressed into pellets of 12mm in diameter and a thickness between 1 and 2 mm with a pressure of 0.9 GPa, loaded in air tight stainless steel cells and contacted between two spring loaded electrodes. Indium foils were mechanically pressed on the pellets surface to improve the contact between the sample and the molybdenum electrodes, for impedance spectroscopy measurements.

Ionic  conductivity  was  measured  by  electrochemical  impedance  spectroscopy  using  a  BioLogic  VMP3 multi-channel potentiostat in  a  frequency  range  from  1 MHz  to  1  Hz  with  a  voltage  amplitude  of  50  mV.  Temperature-dependent measurements were performed by placing the cell in a climatic chamber and equilibrating the cell for 2 h at each temperature. The pellet resistance R was extracted from the last measurement at each temperature, at the intercept with the x-axis of

Cyclic  voltammetry and galvanostatic cycling were conducted using the same BioLogic VMP3 multi-channel potentiostat. Assessment of the oxidative stability was done using a special low-current channel for the VMP3 with a current resolution of 80 fA.
