## Status and prospects of hydroborate electrolytes for all-solid-state batteries

L /C19 eo Duch ^ ene a,b , Arndt Remhof a,* , Hans Hagemann b , Corsin Battaglia a

- a Empa, Swiss Federal Laboratories for Materials Science and Technology, 8600, Dübendorf, Switzerland
- b D /C19 epartement de Chimie-Physique, Universit /C19 e de Gen /C18 eve, 1211 Geneva 4, Switzerland

## A R T I C L E I N F O

Keywords: All-solid-state batteries Solid electrolyte Hydroborates

Closo -borates

## 1. Introduction

Among commercial rechargeable battery systems, lithium-ion batteries deliver the highest speci /uniFB01 c energy, currently up to 200 -250 Wh/kg on the cell level [1]. They enabled the widespread use of portable electronics and are foreseen to play a major role in the fast growing electro-mobility market. The latter requires the development of safer batteries with higher speci /uniFB01 c energy density to greatly extend the driving range of an electric vehicle, and high power density to reduce charging time [2]. However, lithium-ion batteries suffer from intrinsic limitations of the organic liquid electrolyte used in these cells, speci /uniFB01 cally, polarization effects from mixed anion and cation diffusion, i.e. , low cation transference number, which limits the maximum cell current, incompatibility with lithium metal anodes which limits the energy density, and /uniFB02 ammability, requiring safety precautions. The development of batteries improving operational safety that can simultaneously offer high power and high energy density for applications in electro-mobility and stationary energy storage directed the research towards all-solid-state lithium batteries [3,4]. In parallel, sodium based batteries are also considered as a potentially more cost effective alternative [5].

In all-solid-state batteries, the organic liquid electrolyte and porous polymer separator are replaced by a solid ion conductor, which supports fast ion diffusion. Solid ion conductors can be used as a solid electrolyte (SE) in a battery to transfer ions between anode and cathode, given that they meet certain requirements, as illustrated in Fig. 1. First, a SE

## A B S T R A C T

Hydroborates represent a promising, yet underexplored, class of electrolytes for all-solid-state batteries. This article reviews the rapid recent developments that enabled the demonstration of /uniFB01 rst all-solid-state batteries based on hydroborate electrolytes with remarkable cycling stability. High-ionic conductivity, a wide electrochemical stability window, and advantageous mechanical properties make hydroborate electrolytes serious candidates for addressing the major device integration and processing challenges faced by all-solid-state batteries. We also discuss novel approaches for the synthesis of hydroborate compounds from cost-effective feedstock chemicals with low toxicity. Finally, we provide an up-to-date assessment of the key performance metrics of hydroborate electrolytes in comparison to other more established solid electrolyte materials and discuss future research opportunities in the /uniFB01 eld.

requires a high ionic conductivity to enable acceptable charging and discharging rates and it must be selective towards the ion driving the reaction in the considered battery. An ionic conductivity of at least 1 mS cm /C0 1 near room temperature is commonly targeted to compete with established organic liquid electrolytes. Additionally, the electrolyte must be an electrical insulator to avoid self-discharge or short circuiting of the battery. It must also be chemically stable during processing, operation, and storing of the battery and electrochemically stable against oxidation and reduction during charge and discharge. To enable batteries with high energy density, SEs are envisioned for the use of high voltage cathodes such as state-of-the-art transition metal oxides and potentially higher voltage materials (~4.5 -5 V), as well as low voltage, ideally alkali metal anodes. SEs stability, both chemical and electrochemical, in contact with such electrode materials should thus be demonstrated. Also, SEs should possess good deformability and high thermal stability such that they can be processed easily and operated over a wide temperature range without the need of temperature control as for state-of-the-art lithium-ion batteries. Finally, mechanically robust SEs may prevent dendrite formation, allowing the use of metallic anodes to signi /uniFB01 cantly increase the energy density.

Candidate solid electrolytes particularly for lithium-ion and sodiumion batteries attract strong interest from the research community. However, meeting all the aforementioned criteria remains a great challenge [6]. Sul /uniFB01 de-based SEs quickly achieved a high level of device integration thanks to key properties such as high ionic conductivity reaching or even

* Corresponding author. Empa, Swiss Federal Laboratories for Materials Science and Technology, 129 Überlandstrasse, 8600, Dübendorf, Switzerland. E-mail address: arndt.remhof@empa.ch (A. Remhof).

https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ensm.2019.08.032

Received 7 May 2019; Received in revised form 19 August 2019; Accepted 24 August 2019

Available online xxxx

2405-8297/ © 2019 Elsevier B.V. All rights reserved.

Energy Storage Materials xxx (xxxx) xxx

Fig. 1. Schematic structure of an all-solid-state battery comprising a standard composite cathode and a metallic anode. The requirements for the solid electrolyte are depicted in relation to the associated battery component.

overpassing that of conventional liquid electrolyte [7,8], as well as attractive mechanical properties. Still they suffer from a low chemical stability and can decompose in ambient conditions under the release of H2S [9]. Their low electrochemical stability can also require advanced coating methods in conjunction with lithium or sodium metal anodes or high voltage cathodes if the decomposition products do not form a stable passivation layer, analogous to the solid electrolyte interface in organic liquid electrolyte based batteries [10 -15]. Ceramic and polymer ionic conductors are also actively studied. In particular, polymer electrolytes have enabled a high level of device integration, but are not yet competitive with traditional lithium-ion batteries in terms of energy density. We refer the reader to other extensive reviews that discuss properties and perspectives of these types of electrolytes [16 -19].

This review focuses on hydroborate-based solid electrolytes, which are emerging as a promising class of SEs. Particularly exciting results were recently achieved in terms of ionic conductivity and stability, for both lithium and sodium conducting compounds. We /uniFB01 rst review these results and discuss the chemical and structural similarities of these compounds which mainly relate to the characteristics of the hydroborate anions. Other reviews also addressed the promises of hydroborates as energy materials for hydrogen storage, and as ionic conductors [20 -23], while others focused on more fundamental aspects of synthesis or crystal chemistry [24,25].

Still, hydroborates remain relatively underexplored in terms of device integration. In this comprehensive review of recent achievements, we also highlight the practical challenges and research gaps that remain be solved to establish hydroborates as a major contender for next generation all-solid-state batteries. We further discuss new synthetic approaches to render these compounds more readily available for both the research community and to enable scaling to industrial volumes. To provide a broader perspective, we compare the status of hydroborate-based solid electrolytes to other solid electrolyte materials in terms of their physical and chemical properties, but also in terms of their integration into allsolid-state batteries. Finally, we highlight open research challenges associated with this exciting class of compounds.

## 2. Hydroborates as candidate solid electrolyte for all-solid-state batteries

Fast Li þ conductivity in hydroborates was /uniFB01 rst observed in the hightemperature hexagonal phase of LiBH4, which reaches a conductivity of 1 mS cm /C0 1 above 110 /C14 C as a result of a structural phase transition [26]. A Li|LiBH4|LiCoO2 battery could be reversibly cycled at 120 /C14 C with the use of a protective Li3PO4 coating on the LiCoO2 cathode to reduce high interfacial resistance [27]. Several strategies were then employed to increase the room-temperature ionic conductivity of LiBH4. Anion substitutions in solid solutions of LiBH4 and lithium halides, such as LiI, can stabilize the high temperature phase of LiBH4 down to room temperature [28]. Nano-con /uniFB01 nement of LiBH4 or LiBH4 -LiI in porous silica scaffolds also proved to be a successful strategy to further enhance the Li þ conductivity at room temperature [29,30]. In this case the fast Li þ diffusion is attributed to the formation of a nanometer thin interface layer between the hydroborate and SiO2 that does not undergo any structural phase transition [31,32]. However, the conductivity of those materials remains too low to realize a battery with suf /uniFB01 cient rate capability at room temperature [33,34]. A series of compounds with the general formula Li(BH4)1-x(NH2)x with x ranging from 1/2 to 3/4 can reach high room-temperature ionic conductivity around 10 /C0 4 S cm /C0 1 for x ¼ 1/2

L. Duch ^ ene et al.

and x ¼ 3/4 and up to 6.4 mS cm /C0 1 at 40 /C14 C for x ¼ 2/3 [35,36]. This conductivity allowed fast cycling of Li þ between two Li4Ti5O12 electrodes with a performance similar to a liquid-electrolyte-based cell, up to 1C (0.7 mA cm /C0 2 ) at 40 /C14 C. However, Li(BH4)1-x(NH2)x phases suffer from a narrow electrochemical stability window of only 1.3 V (0.7 -2.0 V vs. Li/Li þ ), far too low for integration with high-voltage cathode materials and metallic anodes. In search of higher electrochemical stability, other hydroborates are considered in the following.

Large cage-like BnHn 2 /C0 anions, namely closo -borates, form salts that have been long known for their high chemical and thermal stability [37]. For example, Li2B12H12 is generated as a product of the thermal decomposition of LiBH4 [38]. The electrochemical oxidation of a LiBH4 electrolyte at the interface with a TiS2 cathode is also believed to form a thin and more electrochemically stable layer of Li2B12H12 allowing for the ef /uniFB01 cient cycling of a Li|LiBH4|TiS2 all-solid-state cell [39]. Alkaline closo -borate salts exhibit structural phase transitions accompanied by a reorientational disordering of the anions that is a prerequisite of their high cation conductivity [40 -42]. Such high conductivity was subsequently measured in Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 above 260 /C14 C and 100 /C14 C respectively [43,44], and is expected in Li2B12H12 and Li2B10H10 above 350 /C14 C, but has not been reported at these temperatures due to the decomposition of those compounds [45]. Similarly to simple hydroborates, multiple strategies to lower the transition temperature and trigger high conductivity at room temperature have been pursued. Fig. 2 summarizes these strategies with a few selected examples.

Mechanical milling has been used to stabilize the superionic phase of Li2B12H12 and Na2B12H12 through crystallite size reduction [46]. However, the high ionic conductivity of the milled material is not always stable, as thermal cycling can results in the re-crystallization of the low-temperature phase with low ionic conductivity, as is the case for Li2B12H12.

Chemical modi /uniFB01 cations of the anions, which include atomic substitutions or structure variations, can also improve the ionic conductivity of closo -borates and lower their transition temperature. Metal closo -carbaborates are materials, in which one boron atom of the closo -borate anion is replaced by a carbon atom, resulting in an anion with very similar size and geometry, but with a lower charge as well as a nonnegligible dipolar moment. Consequently, CBn-1Hn /C0 anions interact more weakly with the cations, while the dipolar moment promotes a dynamically /uniFB02 uctuating background energy landscape [47]. This reduces the energy barrier for rotational disordering such that closo -carbaborates compounds exhibit lower transition temperatures and improved ionic conductivities with lower activation energies, compared to their closo -borate counterpart. Unprecedented conductivity in hydroborate compounds was measured in NaCB11H12, LiCB11H12, LiCB9H10 and notably NaCB9H10 [48,49]. In the latter, the conductivity reaches 30 mS cm /C0 1 at room temperature upon cooling (see Fig. 2b) while it is restricted to temperatures above 50 /C14 C upon heating. This is due to a temperature hysteresis in the structural phase transition typically observed for these compounds. Partial substitution of hydrogen atoms by halides also lowers the transition temperature as shown for Na2B12H12-xIx [50]. Full halide substitution however tends to increase the structural transition temperature and restricts high cation conductivity to unpractically high temperatures [51]. Nido -borates are another variation of complex hydroborate materials, in which the anion is monovalent and possesses an open, nest-like structure as opposed to the closed, cage-like structure of closo -borates. As the closo -borates, the nido -borates and nido -carba-borates exhibit structural phase transitions to more symmetric crystal structures with high rotational anion disorder and enhanced cation conductivity [52]. However, the opening of the cage results in reduced thermal stability as well as more pronounced chemical reactivity compared to their nearly aromatic closo -borate counterpart, which could impede their integration into batteries.

Anion substitution and anion mixing has been so far the most successful method to completely suppress the structural phase transition in closo -borates and related compounds. Mixtures of NaBH4 or NaNH2 with Na2B12H12 form new phases with a distinct crystal structure having an open framework of continuous diffusion channels for the cations which reach high ionic conductivity at room temperature [53,54]. Mixing compounds with more closely related anions tend to stabilize the high temperature phase of one or both precursor materials. The equimolar mixture of Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 forms, after mechanical milling and heat treatment, a new phase-pure compound, namely Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) (also previously reported as Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5), having the same face-centered cubic structure as Na2B10H10 above 100 /C14 C, but with slightly expanded lattice parameters [55]. This structure is stable over an extended range of temperature with no structural symmetry changes observed between /C0 170 /C14 C and 450 /C14 C [56]. Consequently, high ionic conductivity is maintained, notably at room temperature (0.9 mS cm /C0 1 at 20 /C14 C), several orders of magnitude above the one of the precursor materials. Other ratios of Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 may also partly stabilize the body-centered structure of Na2B12H12 [46,57]. Similarly, Na2-x(CB11H12)x(B12H12)1-x mixtures crystalize with a body-centered cubic packing, as the high-temperature structure of Na2B12H12 above 260 /C14 C, while the high temperature phase of NaCB9H10 can be stabilized

Fig. 2. Ionic conductivity of selected compounds from Refs. [43,44,46,49] highlighting the different approaches used to stabilize the ionic conductivity of closo --borates at room temperature, speci /uniFB01 cally (a) particle size reduction, (b) chemical modi /uniFB01 cation of the anion and (c) anion substitution. The conductivity of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) was re-measured for this review over an extended temperature range as compared to Ref. [55] The dashed grey lines show the commonly accepted conductivity target of 1 mS cm /C0 1 at room temperature (25 /C14 C) for device relevant ionic conductors.

## L. Duch ^ ene et al.

in Na2(CB11H12)(CB9H10) and the high temperature phases of LiCB11H12 and LiCB9H10 are co-stabilized in Li2(CB11H12)(CB9H10) [58,59]. The latter two compounds reach particularly high conductivity with ~70mScm /C0 1 and ~3 mS cm /C0 1 respectively, at room temperature.

We summarize common key properties, characteristic for hydroborate ionic conductors:

- /C15 High ionic conductivity in pure hydroborate compounds is generally triggered by a structural phase transition constraining access to high ionic conductivity to relatively high temperature, with a few exceptions.
- /C15 These transitions are correlated to a gain in motional degrees of freedom, typically rotational motion of the anions that promotes high cation conductivity.
- /C15 The high temperature crystal structures have high symmetry with a large number of interstitial sites available to the cation within the anion backbone. Fig. 3a shows this behavior for Na2B12H12, a typical example.
- /C15 Finally, the temperature of the structural transition can be reduced or even suppressed by different means as illustrated in Fig. 2.

This behavior differs signi /uniFB01 cantly from other families of ionic conductors and owes largely to the complex geometry and dynamics of the hydroborate ions. While structural aspects such as the crystal packing of the anion sub-lattice were found to be useful criteria to understand ionic conduction in solids with /uniFB01 xed O 2 /C0 or S 2 /C0 anions [60], studies of the conduction mechanism in hydroborate compounds show that the anion backbone does not merely act as a rigid lattice, in which the cations diffuse, but plays a crucial, dynamical role. For example, Li(BH4)0.25(NH2)0.75 possesses a face-centered cubic anion framework, in

Energy Storage Materials xxx (xxxx) xxx which the cations should preferentially diffuse indirectly between tetrahedral interstitial sites by hopping through an octahedral vacancy. This mechanism was shown to be less favorable for sul /uniFB01 de-based ionic conductors when compared to body-centered anion lattices, in which hopping between face-sharing and energetically equivalent tetrahedra is directly possible [60]. However, in Li(BH4)1-x(NH2)x, as calculated using the nudged elastic band method within density functional theory (DFT), Li can directly hop between edge-sharing tetrahedra, avoiding the octahedral vacancy, thanks to anion reorientations. This promotes high ionic conductivity with a low activation energy of about 0.34 eV (see Fig. 3b). Similarly, Na2B12H12-xIx crystalizes in a hexagonal close-packed structure, in which diffusion of the cations also involves octahedral vacancies. Octahedral coordination in Na2B12H12-xIx is not as energetically unfavorable as in sul /uniFB01 de compounds resulting in a very low activation energy of 0.14 eV, lower than any closo -borate ionic conductor and in particular lower than in the high-temperature body-centered cubic phase of Na2B12H12 [50].

This easy access to a high density of interstitial sites in closo -borates is particularly important for their high ionic conductivity as pointed out on the basis of ab initio molecular dynamics simulations [61]. At the origin of this behavior, three types of frustrations have been shown to result in a /uniFB02 attened and dynamically /uniFB02 uctuating energy landscape, as shown in Fig. 3c [62]. First, structural frustrations lead to a low crystallographic site preference for the cations which can diffuse in a high number of different interstitial sites. Second, the competition between local cation-anion coordination and lattice symmetry prevents an ordering of the cation and anion positions. Finally, the dynamical anion rotational disorder further promotes the diffusion of the cations. This disorder caused by the different frustrations may be triggered by temperature and relates to the structural phase transitions observed for these compounds.

Fig. 3. (a) Low (left) and high (right) temperature structure of Na2B12H12 showing the gain in symmetry and motional degrees of freedom typically observed in hydroborate compounds upon temperature increase (reprinted with permission from Ref. [43]. Copyright 2014 The Royal Society of Chemistry). (b) Calculated diffusion pathway for a lithium ion in Li(BH4)0.25(NH2)0.75 showing the active role of the anion backbone and the possibility of direct hoping between edge-sharing tetrahedra thanks to anion reorientations (reprinted with permission from Ref. [36]. Copyright 2017 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA, Weinheim). (c) The combination of structural, chemical and dynamic frustrations leading to high ionic conductivity in closo -borates, as inferred from DFT and ab initio molecular dynamics (reprinted with permission from Ref. [62]. Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society). (d) Typical experimental study of the complex reorientational motion of closo -borate anions by combined NMR and QENS studies (reprinted with permission from Ref. [64]. Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society).

L. Duch ^ ene et al.

Disorder can also be observed down to low temperatures, if an additional element of frustration is present, such as the substitution of a foreign anion or mechanical strain due to crystallite size reduction. Experimentally, it was also shown that these frustrations, while promoting ionic conductivity, are the origin of a pronounced non-Arrhenius temperature dependence of the conductivity, common for these materials [56].

The link between anion rotational disorder and cation diffusion in closo -borates has been extensively studied by means of solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) [40,42,63]. The experimental /uniFB01 nding correlate very well with the aforementioned computational studies. In general, it is found that NMR linewidths remain broad in the low temperature, low conducting phases and narrows signi /uniFB01 cantly upon the structural transition or in stabilized, fast conducting phases [55]. This narrowing is a sign of enhanced mobility and typically occurs simultaneously for the cationic and anionic atomic species con /uniFB01 rming the link between cation and anion dynamics. Similarly, studies of NMR spin-lattice relaxation rates shows that the jump rate for both the anion and the cation are several orders of magnitude higher in the high conducting phases, with the anion jump rate being typically faster than that of the cation. These results are in line with the computational studies of Kweon et al. [62] showing that the role of the anion is to /uniFB02 atten the energy landscape for the cation, promoting their disordering, and that not each anion rotational motion yields a cation jump. In addition, materials with higher cationic conductivity also show a higher jump rate for the anion as found by the comparison of Na2B10H10 and NaCB9H10 [64].

More details about the geometry of the anion reorientations are derived from quasi-elastic neutron scattering experiments, a technique that is particularly sensitive to hydrogen [64 -67]. These experiments con /uniFB01 rmed localized anion motion and thus identify the cations as the dominant contributors to the high ionic conductivity of hydroborates. Anions perform rotational motion, typically through jumps around speci /uniFB01 c axes of symmetry, as depicted in Fig. 3d, which shows the modelled motion of the B10H10 2 /C0 and CB9H10 /C0 anions in their respective lithium and sodium salts.

In summary, there exists a vast library of compounds within the family of hydroborates showing promises as solid ionic conductors. Extensive studies were conducted to understand ionic conduction in these compounds and to achieve high ionic conductivity at and below room temperature. While we have not discussed it in details, solid electrolytes for magnesium batteries based on hydroborates also showed promise for high Mg 2 þ conductivity as well as magnesium stripping and plating [68 -70]. Recently, the focus has shifted to closo -borates and related compounds which have achieved record high conductivity at room temperature up to 70 mS cm /C0 1 for Na2(CB11H12)(CB9H10). Interestingly, high ionic conductivity in closo -borates seems to be more easily achieved for sodium-ion conductors. This may be due to the high polarizability of the sodium ion as pointed out for other ionic conductors [71]. The increasing number of compounds exhibiting high ionic conductivity in the hydroborate family raises the question of their device integration. A number of properties need to be assessed in this regard, notably in terms of the materials stability and compatibilities, including thermal, chemical and electrochemical stability, as detailed in the following section.

## 3. Practical challenges for all-solid-state batteries based on hydroborates

The thermal stability of alkaline hydroborates increases with decreasing electronegativity of the alkaline metal. At one bar of hydrogen, LiBH4 and NaBH4 decompose at 370 and 534 /C14 C respectively [72 -74] Closo -borate compounds, notably B12H12 2 /C0 salts, are often found among the decomposition products of hydroborates. The thermal decomposition of closo -borates thus typically occurs at higher temperature, above 350 /C14 C to more than 600 /C14 C depending on the constituting cation and anion [37,45,75]. In the case of B12H12 2 /C0 compounds, decomposition follows a polymerization process with the slow release of molecular hydrogen [45,75]. In general, closo -borates, possessing a closed-cage structure, are found to have 3D aromaticity which explains this high stability [76,77]. To the best of our knowledge, the thermal stability of closo -carbaborates has not yet been investigated in details. Still, calorimetry measurement in closo -carbaborates based electrolytes showed that these materials are thermally stable up to at least 200 /C14 C [58]. On the other hand, nido -borates with an open-cage structure decompose at a lower temperature, below 200 /C14 C [52]. As all-solid-state batteries are expected to provide higher safety than today ' s lithium-ion batteries, a high thermal stability is particularly attractive. Closo -borates and related compounds stand out in that respect. The hydrogen release upon decomposition, while slow, should be taken into account as it may cause increased /uniFB01 re hazard if the batteries are exposed to extreme temperatures. In particular, the thermal stability of these compounds in air should be evaluated. In addition, the aforementioned studies only refer to the thermal stability of the pure compounds. Tian et al. have pointed out the importance of an in-depth characterization of electrolyte-electrode compatibilities for all-solid-state batteries [11]. The study of the thermal decomposition of hydroborate compounds in the presence of electrode materials may follow a different pathway and has yet to be studied experimentally. Recently, Lacivita et al. have computed the chemical compatibility of sodium-ion conductors, including NaBH4 and Na2B12H12, with different sodium cathode materials [78]. The hydroborates appear to have non-negligible reactivity towards certain oxide cathodes, but these results have yet to be con /uniFB01 rmed by experiment. Still, the recent demonstration of a stable 3 V battery using Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) as a solid electrolyte and a NaCrO2 as cathode shows the compatibility of these materials within a device [79].

While thermal stability is important for the safe operation of all-solidstate batteries, the race for high energy density requires the use of highvoltage cathodes and low voltage, ideally alkali metal, anodes. The (electro-)chemical compatibility between the electrolyte and potential electrode materials should therefore be addressed.

Oxidative stability is usually estimated on the basis of linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) measurements also used to demonstrate the capacity of the electrolyte for lithium or sodium stripping and plating. The current voltage response is thus largely dominated by the metal stripping and plating currents near 0 V and the voltammogram appears to show no decomposition currents up to very large voltages. Large stability windows have thus been initially reported, typically limited to the cutoff voltage of the LSV experiment [33,44,53,54]. Han et al. have pointed out strong discrepancies between measured and calculated oxidation potential for candidate solid electrolytes [80]. Similarly, a 5 V experimental oxidative stability has been reported for LiBH4 while calculations indicate decomposition above ~2 V [10,33,81]. This difference originates in the slow reaction kinetics of the oxidation reaction that yields very small currents and large over potentials when high voltage sweeping rates ( &gt; 1 mV s /C0 1 ) are used during the LSV experiments. More recent evaluation of the electrochemical stability of hydroborate-based materials, performed at much lower voltage sweeping rates (0.1 mV s /C0 1 ) and using an experimental set-up sensitive to very low currents in the nA range, demonstrated the need for more accurate evaluation of the true stability window of hydroborate compounds [55]. Speci /uniFB01 cally, The voltammogram of a Na|Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)|Pt cell shown in Fig. 4a appears /uniFB02 at up to 6.5 V on a mA current scale, while oxidation currents are visible above 3 V on the nA scale. On the same scale, no reduction currents are visible before the sodium plating peaks. A 3 V stability window was thus ascribed to Na4(B12H12)(B10H10), including stability towards sodium metal. This was further con /uniFB01 rmed by the implementation of this electrolyte in a 3 V battery using a NaCrO2 cathode and a sodium metal anode [79]. The battery showed very stable cycling when the upper voltage was limited to 3.25 V, while faster fading was observed when the cell was cycled to 3.6 V, con /uniFB01 rming the previously evaluated stability window. Brighi et al. have applied a different method consisting in mixing carbon black with the Na2-x(CB11H12)x(B12H12)1-x electrolyte to increase the exposed electrolyte surface area during voltammetry measurements [59].

The method enhances the oxidation currents which were clearly visible above 4.1 V vs. Na/Na þ .

The electrochemical stability of some hydroborate compounds was also evaluated on the basis of DFT calculations [10,78,81]. In general, M(BH4)y compounds tend to have a lower stability than their closo -borate counterpart, as shown in Fig. 4b. In fact, one of the electrochemical decomposition products of M(BH4)y compounds is often considered in the calculations to be the corresponding Mx(B12H12) salt. Such a decomposition pathway was also speculated for LiBH4 at the interface with a TiS2 cathode, forming a passivating Li2B12H12 layer [39]. The calculated oxidation stabilities for the closo -borate compounds are in line with that of the most recent experimental work. Closo -borates typically exhibit a large stability window in the 3 -4 V range and show very good reduction stability, for both lithium and sodium compounds. This is in line with the results obtained for both lithium and sodium hydroborate based electrolyte, namely 0.7(LiCB9H10)-0.3(LiCB11H12) and Na4(B12H12)(B10H10), which showed stable cycling with a metal anode in symmetric cells [55,82]. Na2-x(CB11H12)x(B12H12)1-x on the other hand showed improved cycling stability by replacing the sodium metal anode by a Na -Sn alloy with a slightly higher potential near 0.05 V vs. Na/Na þ [59]. While the replacement of the B10H10 2 /C0 anion in Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)

Energy Storage Materials xxx (xxxx) xxx

Fig. 4. (a) Measurement of the electrochemical stability window of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) by cyclic voltammetry highlighting the differences in scale between Na stripping/plating currents compared to oxidation currents (reprinted with permission from Ref. [55]. Copyright 2017 The Royal Society of Chemistry). (b) Predicted stability windows of different hydroborate compounds on the basis of ab initio calculations and, (c) corresponding ternary phase diagram of Na2B12H12 used for the prediction of the stability window (reprinted with permission from Ref. [81]. Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society). (d) Structure of the B24H23 3 /C0 anion, as found from the electrochemical oxidation of the B12H12 2 /C0 anion in solution (from Ref. [83]).

to CB11H12 /C0 in Na2-x(CB11H12)x(B12H12)1-x appears to improve the oxidation stability, this may come at the cost of a slightly reduced reduction stability. Such results highlight the need for a better understanding of the electrochemical properties of the different, individual anions.

/C0

The electrochemistry of hydroborate anions has been studied extensively in solution, many years before these compounds attracted interest as potential solid electrolytes [84]. Among the different BnHn 2 /C0 species with n ¼ 6 -12, the B10H10 2 /C0 and B12H12 2 /C0 anions are found to be the most stable, following the trend of their increasing aromaticity. The addition of a carbon atom or the halogenation of the anion also tends to render them more stable, likely because of the reduced charge density of the anions, which makes them less prone to oxidation. This trend was also recently demonstrated by DFT calculations on halogenated B12HnX(12 2 /C0 anions [85]. This high stability of cage-like hydroborates anions when compared to BH4 /C0 , particularly closo -carbaborate anions, was also noticed for magnesium salts and enabled more stable liquid electrolytes for magnesium batteries [86 -88].

In general, the oxidation of closo -borate and closo -carbaborate compounds is found to occur via the bridging of two anions through the loss of a H þ ion, much like the observed behavior for thermal decomposition n)

## L. Duch ^ ene et al.

[89 -91]. We note that such dimerized compounds were not considered as decomposition products in DFT calculations of the electrochemical stability window of closo -borate compounds. In the case of Na2B12H12 (and Li2B12H12) the main decomposition products are considered to be the neutral B9H11 and elemental boron, as shown in the Na -B -H ternary phase diagram in Fig. 4c. However, Na2B12H12 dissolved in acetonitrile is oxidized to form Na3B24H23 [92]. The structure of the B24H23 3 /C0 anion, later optimized by Rietveld re /uniFB01 nement is shown in Fig. 4d [83,93]. Polymerization products of the decomposition of closo -borates are usually amorphous, as seen during thermal decomposition, which makes their analysis dif /uniFB01 cult, particularly for diffraction studies and ab initio calculations. However, characterization by NMR and infrared spectroscopy is possible and was previously reported. The B -H -B bridging of closo -borate dimers was visible through a speci /uniFB01 c vibrational mode for the decomposition products of both B10H10 2 /C0 and B12H12 2 /C0 [92,94]. Future experiments could thus be designed to identify the electrochemical decomposition pathways of closo -borates and related compounds. The question is then to understand whether a chemically and mechanically stable interface is formed upon the electrolyte decomposition. Like a SEI in an organic liquid electrolyte based battery, this layer should to allow for the passage of ions while blocking electronic currents to avoid further decomposition, thereby making possible the use of hydroborate electrolytes outside of their intrinsic electrochemical stability window. The formation of such SEI was proposed to explain the stable cycling of a TiS2 cathode with a LiBH4 solid electrolyte [39]. More recently evidences for the formation of a SEI were found for a Li4(BH4)3I electrolyte in /uniFB01 ltrated in a mesoporous silica scaffold and allowed for the cycling of all-solid-state cells with a lithium metal anode and various cathode materials [30].

Given their attractive properties in terms of both ionic conductivity and stability, all-solid-state batteries employing closo -borates or closo -carbaborates as the solid electrolyte could be demonstrated for both sodium and lithium as shown in Fig. 5. A /uniFB01 rst step towards device integration is the ability of the electrolyte for reversible metal stripping and plating (Fig. 5a). This was demonstrated for both lithium and sodium closo -borate ionic conductors, namely Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) and 0.7(LiCB9H10)-0.3(LiCB11H12). The latter phase crystalizes in the high temperature phase of LiCB9H10 with a conductivity of 6.7 mS cm /C0 1 at 25 /C14 C [82]. Both sodium and lithium plating were demonstrated via galvanostatic cycling in symmetric cells at 60 /C14 C and 0.1 mA cm /C0 2 in the case of sodium, and at 25 /C14 C and 0.2 mA cm /C0 2 in the case of lithium. In both cases, the alkali metal can be plated with a low over-potential on the order of a few mV thanks to the high conductivity and low interface resistance of these materials. In addition, the measurements were conducted for an extended time period of ~12 days with no signi /uniFB01 cant changes in the over-potential. This shows that the interface between the electrolyte and the alkali metal remains unchanged, i.e. that no electrochemical reaction leading to the build-up of a resistive interlayer is taking place. Only a small increase of the overpotential within each cycle can be observed in the case of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10). This increase is reversible at each cycle and may re /uniFB02 ect a change of the interface area as sodium is stripped and plated. Indeed, a signi /uniFB01 cant amount of charge is transferred at each cycle (12 h cycles at 0.1 mA cm /C0 2 i.e. 1.2 mAh cm /C0 2 ) compared to the test performed with 0.7(LiCB9H10)-0.3(LiCB11H12) (30 min cycles at 0.2 mA cm /C0 2 i.e. 0.1 mAh cm /C0 2 ).

An all-solid-state sodium battery, using a sodium metal anode and NaCrO2 as the cathode, was /uniFB01 rst demonstrated using Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) as solid electrolyte [79]. Careful engineering of the cathode-electrolyte interface allowed for a very stable cycling of the cell which retained 85% of capacity after 250 cycles at C/5. Speci /uniFB01 cally, a fraction of the electrolyte was dried onto the cathode particles from a methanol solution, yielding an intimate and stable electrode-electrolyte interface and showing promise for future solution-processing of these compounds. More recently, a lithium sulfur battery was demonstrated using 0.7(LiCB9H10)-0.3(LiCB11H12) as electrolyte. The cell was stably cycled at room temperature up to a rate of 1C and 5C at 60 /C14 C, for the discharge rate, while the charging rate was always kept at a maximum of 1C. This corresponds to a high current density of about 4 mA cm /C0 2 calculated from the reported mass loading and electrode size. Remarkably, such a high current density could be applied without evidence of dendrite formation at the anode.

We note that while a solution based electrolyte-on-electrode coating process had to be implemented for the stable cycling of a Na| Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)|NaCrO2 cell, this was not necessary for the Li| 0.7(LiCB9H10)-0.3(LiCB11H12)|S cell. Instead, a large weight ratio (50 wt %) of electrolyte was used in the composite cathode of the latter. In addition, the cells using sodium metal could not be cycled at high rates or room temperature because of the formation of dendrites, while dendrite formation was not a problem in the lithium metal cell. These aspects raise the question of not only the chemical but also mechanical stability of the different interfaces. Mechano-chemical challenges concern not only hydroborate-based all-solid-state batteries, but also other established class of electrolytes [95,96]. In this regard, recent work has pointed out attractive mechanical properties of closo -borate compounds, notably their low bulk modulus [81,97]. The bulk modulus of closo -borates is found to be on the order of 10 -20 GPa, on the basis of DFT calculations, while an experimental value of 14 GPa has been determined for a high pressure polymorph of Na2B12H12 [98]. Such low bulk moduli, lower than most other families of ionic conductors [99], are associated with high compressibility, essential for a material to accommodate volume changes during battery cycling [96]. This is also an advantage for battery assembly as dense pellets of electrolyte layers may be assembled by simple cold-pressing [44]. The low bulk modulus of hydroborate compounds is however associated with a low shear modulus on the order of or lower than that of the corresponding alkali metal which may be detrimental, notably to prevent dendrite penetration [100].

While other cells using TiS2 as cathode were also demonstrated for several hydroborate compounds [48,57], the two aforementioned cells show the potential of closo -borates for all-solid-state battery applications as they demonstrate the use of both 3 V class transition metal oxide cathode or high energy sulfur cathode, in combination with an alkali metal anode. Still, the total mass loading and active material content of these cells remains relatively low ( &lt; 5 mg cm /C0 2 ) and further work remains to be done to truly match the high expectations associated with all-solid-state batteries in terms of energy and power density.

## 4. Synthetic approaches for closo -borates

Presently, the high cost of the closo -borates and related compounds is a major challenge on the way to a competitive battery. Commercial sources are scarce, and Na2B10H10 is sold for about 220 € /g. For practical applications, the synthesis of closo -borate compounds must therefore be improved signi /uniFB01 cantly to become commercially viable. In the review of Hansen et al., [24] several approaches to synthesize BnHn 2 /C0 species (n ¼ 10 and n ¼ 12) have been summarized. Noted that some of these procedures yield mixtures of compounds due to the high temperature reactions involved. These approaches are the following:

- /C15 reaction of BH4 /C0 with diborane (toxic gas)
- /C15 reaction of BH4 /C0 with iodine forming in a /uniFB01 rst step B3H8 /C0 , and then upon heating B12H12 2 /C0 and BH4 /C0
- /C15 reactions with decaborane (B10H14) as starting material (also toxic), which can produce B10H10 2 /C0 in dimethylsul /uniFB01 de in the presence of NH3 gas.

A different synthesis of Na2B12H12 had been reported by Harzdorf et al. [101] who reacted dry borax with sodium (and aluminum) metal at high temperatures (600 /C14 C) under hydrogen gas at 3 bar:

Depending on the reaction conditions, partially chlorinated B12H12-nCln 2 /C0 with n /C20 6 can also be formed this way.

More recently, He et al. [103 -105] reconsidered reactions with decaborane in the absence of solvents. In a /uniFB01 rst study, the reaction with MBH4 and MH (M ¼ Li, Na, K) was compared:

This reaction with MBH4 takes place between 200 /C14 C and 450 /C14 C for M ¼ Li, Na, and K under argon atmosphere, and proceeds completely as shown by vibrational, NMR and XRD analysis. The reaction with the binary metal hydride MH also takes place at high temperatures, but less ef /uniFB01 ciently than the reaction with MBH4 compounds. In a recent work, Toyama et al. showed that increasing the ratio of LiBH4 to B10H14 can

This mixed compound with the structure of the parent Li2B12H12 reaches a superionic conductivity of 0.79 S cm /C0 1 at 550 K, which is signi /uniFB01 cantly higher than the conductivity of Na2B12H12 and Li2B12H12.

Recently, He et al. [105] published a new solvent free method to prepare NaBH4 and Na2B12H12 from the reactions:

The ratio of the two starting products in /uniFB02 uences the reaction products, and intermediate compositions mixtures of NaBH4 and Na2B12H12 can be obtained. Finally, a controlled decomposition of LiBH4 under H2 pressure, and without B10H14 can also yield a mixture of hydrogen de /uniFB01 cient Li2B12H12, LiH and residual LiBH4 with a high room temperature conductivity [107]. However such decomposition of LiBH4 involves competing decomposition pathways and the product purity may be hard to control, particularly in view of production upscaling [108].

Fig. 6. Selected synthesis routes for several complex hydroborates anions, starting from the BH4 /C0 anion. For each route, the cation, solvent, or other reactant and temperature of the main reaction are given, when available. ## L. Duch ^ ene et al.

Considering these previously reported syntheses, and taking into account that both diborane and decaborane are highly reactive and toxic and thereby expensive to handle, new synthesis routes are now being explored to yield closo -borates and related compounds from cheaper and easily available precursors. In particular, we would like to highlight that several closo -borate compounds can be obtained starting from cheap NaBH4, a readily available and commonly used reducing agent. The routes from BH4 /C0 to the more complex hydroborate anions are highlighted in Fig. 6.

Moury et al. [109] developed a new procedure to synthesize closo --borate ions. The strategy for this approach /uniFB01 rst involves the clean production of B3H8 /C0 ions starting from BH4 /C0 , and in a second step the production of B12H12 2 /C0 in a controlled way. In a /uniFB01 rst approach [109], Na2B12H12 was obtained from the following set of reactions (TBA ¼ tetrabutylammonium (n-C4H9)4N þ ):

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

TBAB3H8 was then converted into the sodium salt:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

NaB3H8 can also be prepared directly by reacting NaBH4 with benzyl chloride C7H7Cl in anhydrous diglyme at 100 /C14 C [110]. The NaB3H8 sodium salt is then heated to 165 /C14 C in diglyme to form a precipitate of Na2B12H12. Alternatively, TEAB3H8 (TEA ¼ tetraethylammonium (C2H5)4N þ ) can be converted to TEA2B10H10 at 185 /C14 C in dry conditions [111].

The synthesis of CB11H12 /C0 /uniFB01 rst requires the preparation of the nido -ion B11H14 /C0 , which can be achieved by various methods [112 -114]. In particular, the reaction between NaBH4 and BF3.Et2O produces nido -B11H14 /C0 with a yield of about 50% [114,115]. NaB3H8 is believed to be a /uniFB01 rst intermediate of this reaction and has been shown to yield equally nido -B11H14 /C0 with BF3.Et2O [115]. The subsequent reaction with CHCl3 produces the closo -ion CB11H12 /C0 with a 40% yield [116]. This yield was recently increased to 95% by using CF3Si(CH3)3 in place of CHCl3, in a reaction that could be scaled to produce nearly 15 g of product [117]. nido -B11H14 /C0 can also be converted into decaborane(14) (B10H14), which is also a starting product to form the closo -borates B12H12 2 /C0 , as discussed above [114,118].

An interesting route for the preparation of closo -carbaborate anion involves the thermal decomposition of CsCB10H13 which can yield the CB11H12 /C0 and CB9H10 /C0 ion simultaneously [119]:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

The reported synthesis of CsCB10H13 however involves the use of both B10H14 and NaCN [120]. In general the preparation of CB9H10 /C0 is more challenging and only synthesis based on B10H14 have been reported. For example, Ringstrand et al. synthesized CsCB9H10 with a yield of 29% in an optimized Brellochs reaction of B10H14 with aldehydes [121].

In summary, while historical routes for the synthesis of closo -borates involved the use of toxic precursor materials, recent work has shown that synthesis from cheap precursor with much reduced toxicity can be achieved. In particular, the closo -borate anions can be obtained from readily available precursors like NaBH4. We also note that while precursor materials like decaborane are known to be toxic [122], the salt of the B12H12 2 /C0 and B10H10 2 /C0 anions have been reported to have a low acute toxicity for rats [37]. The synthesis of closo -carbaborates is more challenging, though new routes through the closing of nido -borates anions appear quite promising.

## 5. Conclusion &amp; perspectives

With regard to the criteria that must be ful /uniFB01 lled by solid electrolytes, hydroborate and particularly closo -borates and related closo -carbaborates stand out, not only within the hydroborate family but also compared to the other established classes of solid electrolytes, as shown in Fig. 7. Considering only materials properties, these compound exhibit a particularly promising combination of high ionic conductivity, high thermal, chemical and electrochemical stability as well as favorable mechanical properties in view of device integration.

Still, research gaps remain to be /uniFB01 lled. Speci /uniFB01 cally, while /uniFB01 rst devices with promising performance were reported, studies on device integration are still at an early stage when compared to more established materials classes. In addition, reliable commercial sources for these compounds are very scarce and thus their price is high. In this aspect, we have highlighted that synthetic approaches involving cheap and non-toxic precursor exist, but need to be further established within the battery community.

In summary, research on hydroborate-based solid electrolytes matured to a point where the conductivities as well as (electro-)chemical stability are suf /uniFB01 cient to build all-solid-state batteries. The performance of /uniFB01 rst devices has quickly reached a level comparable of that of more established materials. Thus, similarly to these other material classes, the

Fig. 7. Performance properties of four major classes of solid electrolytes, including hydroborates. For each material class, some of the major advantages and disadvantages are given.

## L. Duch ^ ene et al.

challenges faced by hydroborate electrolytes have shifted towards their mechano-chemical integrity during device operation. We envision that hydroborate materials could play a signi /uniFB01 cant role to answer to these challenges.

Part IV

## CONCLUSION &amp; PERSPECTIVES

In this thesis we have evaluated the potential of hydrocloso -borate as solid electrolytes for all-solid-state batteries. At the start of this work, preliminary studies had shown the potential of these compounds for high ionic conductivity at high temperature, notably for sodium-ion conduction in Na 2 B 12 H 12 and Na 2 B 10 H 10 . This family of material had also been noticed more than 50 years ago for their stability, found to be "truly surprising when contrasted to that of other boron hydrides."[ 1 ] Hydroborate materials also contain light atoms and thereby exhibit a low density which can be beneficial to reduce the battery weight.

In this work we have used these properties as a starting point to develop a new ionic conductor, namely Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ). This materials reaches an ionic conductivity at room temperature near that of conventional organic liquid based electrolyte. In the perspective of high power devices as discussed in the Introduction we showed that such conductivity, combined with an appropriate engineering of the electrolyte-electrode interfaces, can be sufficient to charge a NaCrO 2 cathode at a rate of 5 C (12 min charge) with more than 60 % of the theoretical capacity. This result is a confirmation of the potential of solid electrolytes in general and closo -borates in particular for fast charging. Further improvements could be obtained by reducing the thickness of the electrolyte to reduce the overall cell resistance. Tuning of the electrolyte composition could also allow to reach even higher ionic conductivity (e. g. by doping with a divalent cation or monovalent anion to create vacancies in the Na + diffusion network).

We also evaluated the electrochemical stability window of the electrolyte that reaches 3 V vs. Na/Na + . Importantly, this includes stability towards sodium metal and gives perspective for high energy devices as discussed in Chapter 2 . The use of Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) with a sodium metal anode was tested in symmetric cells as well as in devices including a NaCrO 2 cathode. The results showed that the sodium metal anode could be used at 60 ◦ C with relatively low current densities. The challenge remains to develop a working sodium metal battery at room temperature and with high current densities. Indeed, under these conditions, the use of a sodium metal anode is not yet possible because of inhomogeneous sodium plating during the charge of the battery similarly to what is observed for liquid electrolytes. Therefore, the solid electrolyte is not yet able to fully enable a functional sodium metal anode. We would like to stress that this issue is common for many ionic conductors including ceramic materials. A fundamental understanding of sodium (and lithium) metal anodes is still needed to fully enable their potential.[ 2 , 3 ]

Another route to increase the energy density of future batteries using e. g. Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ), is to use a cathode with a working potential near 4 V or higher. In this work we have demonstrated the feasibility of 3 V devices using Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ). We also showed

that higher voltage cycling lead to a faster fading of the cell. In view of a future development of this material toward high voltage devices, a fundamental understanding of the oxidation pathways of closo -borates is thus needed. Such investigations are required to understand whether a passivating layer can be formed, which can block further reaction at the interface between the electrolyte and the cathode. If not such a passivating layer will have to be prepared prior to battery assembly, e. g. by coating the cathode material.

The performance of the devices tested with Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) also owes to the ability of this electrolyte to be solution processed. It helps to provide an intimated contact between the electrolyte and the electrode material which is instrumental to the cycling stability and rate capability. Importantly, solution processing can be more easily combined to existing battery fabrication protocols for a faster and cheaper adoption of solid-state technology in industry. In this thesis we mainly work with a NaCrO 2 cathode which is known to have a low volume change upon electrochemical cycling. Further test should thus be performed to assess the mechanical stability of interfaces with other active materials that may exhibit a larger volume change. In general, the mechanical behavior of interfaces in all-solid-state batteries remain an open challenge for most classes of materials.[ 4 ]

We have also confirmed that closo -borates exhibit high thermal stability up to 450 ◦ C. This provides a significant safety margin as compared to conventional organic liquid electrolytes in view of a potential high safety of future all-solid-state devices. Further measurements should also be done to consider the reactivity of these compounds in contact with the active materials in a device and to precisely assess their flammability.

Finally, while the synthesis of closo -borate is not yet established on a large scale, which means that they remain expensive, we discussed promising synthesis route based on inexpensive and easily available precursors. A low price of synthesis combined to the ease of processing is a significant technology enabler for a future all-solid-state battery technology.

Figure 10 . 1 : Radar plots for the key metrics for solid electrolytes where a high performance is shown on the outer part of the plot. The metrics are evaluated for closo -borate electrolytes before and after this work and in the perspective of future improvements.

If not all the challenges have been solved in this work, it demonstrates the great potential of closo -borate electrolytes for all-solid-state batteries. More details and perspectives on this family of materials are also given in Article V. In summary, the combination of high-ionic conductivity, a wide electrochemical stability window, and advantageous mechanical properties make closo -borate electrolytes in general, and Na 4 (B 12 H 12 )(B 10 H 10 ) in particular serious candidates for addressing the major device integration and processing challenges faced by all-solid-state batteries.

## ANALYTICAL APPROXIMATION TO THE COUPLING MODEL

## a . 1 model derivation

The relaxation function defined by parts in the CM is not analytical at t = t c . It is only continuous up to the first derivative which can cause problems for fitting. The first step of the model is to rewrite the two relaxation function with the simplest analytical function that has the correct asymptotic limit t = t c :

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where r = t c / t 0. From this relation, one can extract the average relaxation time characteristic of the diffusion process as:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where G ( a , b ) is the upper incomplete gamma function. The average relaxation time can be use to define several useful materials function notably the permittivity # ( w ) and the conductivity s ( w ) . In particular some useful asymptotic values are:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where 〈 t 〉 is defined above. 〈 t 2 〉 is defined as ∫ ¥ 0 t · c ( t ) dt . In the model, # ( w → 0 ) is further simplified as:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where G ( p ) = G ( p , 0 ) is the complete Gamma function.

At low frequency, i. e. w glyph[lessmuch] 〈 t 〉 -1 the imaginary part of the electric modulus can then be approximated as:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

with # ( 0 ) given by Eq. (A. 5 ).

At high frequency, i. e. w glyph[greatermuch] 〈 t 〉 -1 the electric modulus can be approximated as:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where ˜ xm glyph[similarequal] [ G ( 1 + b )] 1/ b and ts = t c ∗ ( r / b ) -1/ b . Note that Mhigh ( w ) is a complex function. In the model only the imaginary part is used to fit M ′′ ( w ) . Then the low and high frequency parts are combined with a low and high pass filter:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

where w t is freely adjusted.

This model can fit experimental data of the imaginary part of M ( w ) where w is the angular frequency, i. e. w = 2 p f , f being the frequency set by the instrument. The fitting parameters are r , b , # ¥ and w t while t c is fixed equal to 2 ps. To get a more accurate fit it is recommended to fit log data for both the modulus and the frequency. Indeed both of these values can span several orders of magnitude and log data allow to give the same weight for the entire data range.

## a . 2 igor code

The model has been implemented in IgorPro with the following code.

```
#pragma TextEncoding = "UTF -8 " #pragma rtGlobals=3 // Use modern global access method and strict wave access. //This is a fit function implementing a model to fit electric modulus data (imaginary part). The model is based on DOI :10.1016/j.electacta.2018.11.082. //The function has 4 fitting parameter : e _ inf, r, b and w _ t and work for log(M'') plotted against log(angular _ frequency) //To use it go to Analysis-->Curve Fitting and select Modulus _ fit _ log in the list of available function. Then select the data range on which to fit the function. //////////////////////////////////////////////////////// return (t _ c/r) * (r/b)^(1-1/b) * exp(r/b) * gammaInc(1/b,r/b)
```

```
Function tau _ average(r,b) Variable r,b Variable t _ c=2e-12 End Function tau _ sigma(r,b)
```

```
Variable r,b Variable t _ c=2e-12 return t _ c * (b/r)^(1/b) End Function x _ m(b) Variable b return (gamma(1+b))^(1/b) End Function epsilon _ 0(e _ inf,b,r) Variable e _ inf,b,r Variable t _ c=2e-12 return e _ inf * b * gamma(2/b)/(gamma(1/b)^2) End //////////////////////////////////////////////////////// Low and High frequency contributions Function low _ freq(e _ inf,r,b,w) Variable e _ inf,r,b,w return ((1/e _ inf) * tau _ average(r,b) * w)/(1+(epsilon _ 0(e _ inf,b,r) * tau _ average(r,b) * w)^2) End Function high _ freq(e _ inf,r,b,w) Variable e _ inf,r,b,w variable /c i=cmplx(0,1) return (1/e _ inf) * exp(r/b) * imag(exp(-(x _ m(b)/(i * w * tau _ sigma(r,b))) ^b)) End //////////////////////////////////////////////////////// Filters Function Low _ pass(w,w _ t) Variable w,w _ t return (1/(1+(w/w _ t)^2)) End Function High _ pass(w,w _ t) Variable w,w _ t return ((w/w _ t)^2)/(1+(w/w _ t)^2) ENd //////////////////////////////////////////////////////// Fit Function Function Modulus _ fit _ log(w0,w) : FitFunc Wave w0 Variable w Variable lf,lp,hf,hp,result //CurveFitDialog/ Independent Variables 1 //CurveFitDialog/ w //CurveFitDialog/ Coefficients 4 //CurveFitDialog/ w0[0] = w _ t //CurveFitDialog/ w0[1] = e _ inf //CurveFitDialog/ w0[2] = r //CurveFitDialog/ w0[3] = b
```

```
lp=Low _ pass(10^w,10^w0[0]) lf=low _ freq(w0[1],w0[2],w0[3],10^w) hp=High _ pass(10^w,10^w0[0]) hf=high _ freq(w0[1],w0[2],w0[3],10^w) if ((lp * lf+hp * hf)>0) result=log(lp * lf+hp * hf) endif return result End
```
